A lead-acid traction battery in an electric road car lasts about 700 cycles, or roughly five years. Industrial trucks like fork-lifts can get 1200–1500 cycles over 7–8 years, because their batteries are drained more slowly (7–8 hours instead of about 2 hours in cars).
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.3.3 Battery Life and Maintenance · Page: 57
"the life of the lead acid battery is limited to around 700 cycles, though this strongly depends on the depth of the cycles."
Basic w-batt-021
What is a NiCad battery?
The nickel-cadmium (NiCad) battery uses nickel oxyhydroxide for the positive electrode and metallic cadmium for the negative electrode. It has roughly twice the specific energy of lead-acid, around 2500 cycles of life, and works from −40 to 80°C. Each cell gives a nominal 1.2 V.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.4.2 Nickel Cadmium · Page: 59
"these batteries have nearly twice the specific energy of lead acid batteries... The NiCad battery uses a nickel oxyhydroxide for the positive electrode and metallic cadmium for the negative electrode."
Basic w-batt-022
Why is cadmium a problem in NiCad batteries?
Cadmium is expensive — several times the cost of lead — and is unlikely to get cheaper. It is also environmentally harmful and carcinogenic. Together, these drawbacks make NiCad batteries pricier and less attractive than the cadmium-free NiMH alternative.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.4.2 Nickel Cadmium · Page: 59
"the cost of cadmium is several times that of lead, and this is not likely to change. Cadmium is also environmentally harmful and carcinogenic."
Basic w-batt-023
What is a nickel-metal hydride (NiMH) battery?
A NiMH battery is similar in performance to a NiCad battery, but the negative electrode stores hydrogen in a metal hydride instead of using cadmium. It avoids the toxic cadmium problem, has specific energy around 65 Wh/kg, and can be charged in about one hour.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.4.3 Nickel Metal Hydride Batteries · Page: 61
"in the NiMH battery the negative electrode uses hydrogen, absorbed in a metal hydride, which makes it free from cadmium, a considerable advantage."
Basic w-batt-024
Why does a NiMH battery often have cooling fans?
NiMH batteries can be charged so quickly that they get hot. Besides the normal heat from internal resistance, the reaction in which hydrogen bonds to the metal electrode is strongly exothermic. So most NiMH packs for EVs include integral cooling fans to keep temperatures safe.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.4.3 Nickel Metal Hydride Batteries · Page: 62
"the reaction in which hydrogen is bonded to the metal adjacent to the negative electrode is quite strongly exothermic... a cooling system is an important feature of NiMH battery systems."
Basic w-batt-025
What temperature does a sodium sulfur battery run at?
Sodium sulfur batteries operate at very high temperatures, between 300°C and 350°C. They use molten sodium for the negative electrode and molten sulfur for the positive electrode, with a solid beta-alumina ceramic acting as both electrolyte and barrier between the two.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.5.2 Sodium Sulfur Batteries · Page: 64
"they run at temperatures between 300 and 350 ◦C."
Basic w-batt-026
What is a ZEBRA (sodium metal chloride) battery?
The ZEBRA battery uses solid nickel chloride as the positive electrode and molten sodium as the negative electrode, working around 320°C. It has very high specific energy and is much safer than the sodium sulfur battery, even passing rigorous crash tests at 50 kph.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.5.3 Sodium Metal Chloride (ZEBRA) Batteries · Page: 65
"The Zebra cell uses solid nickel chloride for the positive electrode and molten sodium for the negative electrode."
Basic w-batt-027
Why must a ZEBRA battery be kept hot when parked?
The ZEBRA battery only works at around 320°C, so it must stay hot or it will solidify. Unless the car is parked only briefly, the battery needs to stay plugged in to a mains supply when not in use, just to keep itself at operating temperature.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.5.3 Sodium Metal Chloride (ZEBRA) Batteries · Page: 65
"unless it is for a very short period, a few hours, these batteries need to be kept connected to a mains supply when not in use. This is to keep the battery hot"
Basic w-batt-028
What is a lithium-ion battery?
A lithium-ion battery is a rechargeable battery in which lithium ions move from the negative electrode (usually carbon/graphite) to the positive electrode (a metal oxide) during discharge, and back during charging. The flow of electrons through the external circuit produces useful electric current.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.6.3 The Lithium Ion Battery · Page: 69
"A lithium ion battery is a rechargeable battery in which lithium ions move between the anode and cathode, creating a flow of electricity."
Basic w-batt-029
What materials are inside a typical Li-ion cell?
The three main parts of a lithium-ion cell are the anode (commonly graphite carbon), the cathode (a metal oxide such as lithium cobalt oxide, lithium iron phosphate, or lithium manganese oxide), and the electrolyte (a lithium salt dissolved in an organic solvent).
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.6.3 The Lithium Ion Battery · Page: 69
"The three primary functional components of a lithium ion battery are the anode, cathode and electrolyte. The anode of a conventional lithium ion cell is made from carbon, the cathode is a metal oxide, and the electrolyte is a lithium salt in an organic solvent."
Basic w-batt-030
Why must Li-ion charging voltage be controlled precisely?
Lithium-ion cells need accurate voltage control while charging. If the charging voltage is slightly too high, the battery can be damaged; if it is too low, the battery will not become fully charged. So suitable, smart commercial chargers are essential for Li-ion EV packs.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.6.3 The Lithium Ion Battery · Page: 69
"accurate control of voltage is needed when charging lithium cells. If it is slightly too high it can damage the battery; too low and the battery will be insufficiently charged."
Basic w-batt-031
What is a metal-air battery?
A metal-air battery cannot be recharged simply by reversing the current. Instead, the spent metal electrodes are physically replaced and the electrolyte refreshed. The used material is then sent to a reprocessing plant where it is recovered for new electrodes, much like fuel.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.7.1 Introduction · Page: 69
"the batteries cannot be recharged simply by reversing the current. Instead the spent metal electrodes must be replaced or reprocessed."
Basic w-batt-032
What is a zinc-air battery?
A zinc-air battery has a solid zinc negative electrode, a porous positive electrode where oxygen from the air reacts, and a liquid alkaline electrolyte. It has a much higher specific power than the aluminium-air battery, which makes it suitable for use in road vehicles such as buses.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.7.3 The Zinc–Air Battery · Page: 70
"The zinc–air battery is similar in many ways to the aluminium– air battery but has a much better overall performance – particularly with regard to specific power, which is nearly 10 times that of the aluminium air battery"
Basic w-batt-033
What is a supercapacitor?
A supercapacitor (or ultracapacitor) is a very large capacitor used as an energy store. It has high specific power but low specific energy — meaning it can absorb or release energy very quickly but cannot store large amounts. A 4000 F unit can fit inside a beer-can-sized container.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.8.1 Supercapacitors · Page: 71
"Supercapacitors or ultracapacitors are large capacitors which can be can be used as energy stores. They are devices which have high specific power and low specific energy."
Basic w-batt-034
Why do supercapacitor banks need charge-equalisation circuits?
Each supercapacitor can only hold a low voltage (2–3 V), so many must be wired in series. Tiny differences in self-discharge cause some cells to hold more voltage than others. Charge-equalisation circuits monitor adjacent cells and move charge between them so no cell exceeds its safe voltage.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.8.1 Supercapacitors · Page: 73
"The only solution to this, and it is essential in systems of more than about six capacitors in series, is to have charge equalisation circuits."
Basic w-batt-035
What is a flywheel as an EV energy store?
A flywheel is a spinning disc that stores kinetic energy. The energy is released when the flywheel slows down. The flywheel can be reaccelerated by braking, acting as a very efficient regenerative brake, and can be coupled to wheels by a generator or a mechanical gearbox.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.8.2 Flywheels · Page: 73
"Flywheels are devices that are used for storing energy. A plane disc spinning about its axis would be an example of a simple flywheel. The kinetic energy of the spinning disc is released when the flywheel slows down."
Indirect w-batt-036
Why is lead-acid still widely used despite its low specific energy?
Lead-acid is the cheapest rechargeable battery per kilowatt-hour stored and is built from cheap materials. It has a long track record, reliable performance and a wide range of efficient specific powers. For short-range vehicles like golf carts and wheelchairs, no better choice exists today.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.10.2 Batteries which are Currently Available Commercially · Page: 80
"lead acid is cheap and for uses not requiring large amounts of energy storage; for example, for short-range vehicles such as golf carts and wheelchairs, which can be charged overnight, there is no better choice of battery."
Indirect w-batt-037
Why is lithium-ion now the preferred EV battery?
Lithium-ion batteries have specific energy around three times that of lead-acid, high energy density and good specific power, all in a smaller, lighter pack. Prices have dropped enough that big manufacturers now mass-produce EVs, like the Nissan Leaf, Mitsubishi MiEV, Tesla Roadster and Chevrolet Volt with Li-ion.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.6.3 The Lithium Ion Battery · Page: 69
"The LIB has a considerable weight advantage over other battery systems and this makes it a highly attractive candidate for EVs. The specific energy, for example, is about three times that of lead acid batteries"
Indirect w-batt-038
Why does NiMH self-discharge faster than NiCad?
Hydrogen molecules are very small and can easily diffuse through the electrolyte to the positive electrode, where they react with the nickel oxyhydroxide. This slowly drains the cell. As a result, NiMH cells suffer noticeably faster self-discharge than the more stable NiCad cells.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.4.3 Nickel Metal Hydride Batteries · Page: 62
"Hydrogen molecules are very small, and they can reasonably easily diffuse through the electrolyte to the positive electrode... The result is that this battery is subject to quite rapid self-discharge."
Indirect w-batt-039
Why is the ZEBRA battery a poor fit for autonomous parked hybrids?
A ZEBRA battery needs to stay at about 320°C, which drains about 2.4 kWh (~13% of its stored energy) per day just for heating. So it must stay plugged in, which is impractical for a hybrid that may sit in an airport car park for two weeks.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.10.2 Batteries which are Currently Available Commercially · Page: 81
"the fact that it needs to be kept hot is a major drawback to its use in IC engine/electric hybrids, as these are largely totally autonomous vehicles which may be left unused for long periods"
Indirect w-batt-040
Why can't an aluminium-air battery power a road car on its own?
The aluminium-air battery has extremely low specific power: a 100 kg pack gives only about 1000 W. To get 20 kW you would need 2 tonnes of battery. So it is unsuitable as the sole power source for a road vehicle, though it could be used as a base load.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.7.2 The Aluminium–Air Battery · Page: 70
"The big drawback of the aluminium –air battery is its extremely low specific power. For example, a 100 kg battery would give only 1000 W"
Indirect w-batt-041
Why is charge equalisation important for battery life?
Cells in a battery self-discharge at slightly different rates. Without equalisation, weaker cells fall lower with each cycle until one is fully flat. The pack then drives current backwards through it and ruins the cell. One damaged cell ruins the whole series battery.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.9.2 Charge Equalisation · Page: 78
"Because a battery is a series circuit, one damaged cell ruins the whole battery. This effect is probably the major cause of premature battery failure."
Indirect w-batt-042
Why does the last bit of charging take much longer than the first?
Once most cells are nearly full, weaker cells still need to catch up. They can only be safely overcharged at a small current (about C/10). So the charger must keep trickling in current for hours to bring those laggard cells up to fully charged.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.9.2 Charge Equalisation · Page: 78
"the final process of bringing all the cells up to fully charged cannot be done quickly. This explains why it takes so much longer to charge a battery fully than to take it to nearly full."
Indirect w-batt-043
Why are spinning flywheels considered dangerous?
A high-speed flywheel stores a lot of kinetic energy. If it ruptures in a crash, it releases that energy almost instantly, acting like a bomb. If it tears loose from its mountings it can also cause real damage. Plus, gyroscopic effects can affect the vehicle's handling.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.8.2 Flywheels · Page: 76
"In the event of the flywheel rupturing, during a crash, energy is released almost instantly and the flywheel effectively acts like a bomb."
Indirect w-batt-044
Why can't a flywheel store as much energy as we want?
Energy stored in a flywheel increases with its rotational speed squared. But as speed rises, internal stress in the disc also rises. Eventually the flywheel material's tensile strength sets a hard limit — beyond it the disc would burst. So energy storage is fundamentally capped.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.8.2 Flywheels · Page: 75
"as the flywheel's rotational speed increases so do the stresses in the material. As a result the flywheel's energy storage capacity is limited by the tensile strength of the material it is made from."
Indirect w-batt-045
Why would you pair a zinc-air battery with a flywheel?
Zinc-air batteries can't be recharged on the vehicle, so they cannot directly accept regenerative braking energy. But pairing one with a flywheel lets the flywheel absorb the braking energy quickly and feed it back during acceleration, giving the vehicle a working regenerative-braking system.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.11.4 Combinations using Flywheels · Page: 81
"the zinc–air battery cannot be recharged in its location on the vehicle, and hence cannot be used for regenerative braking, but by combining this with a suitable flywheel a vehicle using a zinc– air battery with regenerative braking could be designed."
Medium w-batt-046
What is Peukert's law and what does the Peukert coefficient mean?
Peukert's law links battery capacity to discharge current with the formula Cp = (I^k)·T, where k is the Peukert coefficient. The lower k is, the less capacity falls when current rises. For lead-acid, k is typically about 1.2; other types tend to be lower.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.12.3 Modelling Battery Capacity · Page: 86
"there is a capacity, called the Peukert capacity, which is constant and is given by the equation... where k is a constant (typically about 1.2 for a lead acid battery) called the Peukert coefficient."
Medium w-batt-047
How is the internal resistance of a lead-acid battery estimated?
For a good-quality lead-acid traction battery, internal resistance is approximately 0.022 ohms per cell, scaled inversely by the amp-hour capacity. So for a 12 V (six-cell) battery rated 100 Ah at the 10-hour rate, R is roughly (0.022 × 6) ÷ 100 ohms.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.3.1 Lead Acid Battery Basics · Page: 55
"The figure given in Table 3.1 of 0.022 per cell is a rule of thumb taken from a range of good-quality traction batteries."
Medium w-batt-048
How can the Peukert coefficient be calculated from two ratings?
Take two capacity ratings at different discharge times — for example 42 Ah at 10 hours and 33.6 Ah at 1 hour. Convert to currents, then use k = (log T₁ − log T₂) ÷ (log I₂ − log I₁). The Peukert coefficient is the slope of log T versus log I.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.12.5 Calculating the Peukert Coefficient · Page: 91
"This equation allows us to calculate the Peukert coefficient k for a battery, provided we have two values for the capacity at two different discharge times T."
Medium w-batt-049
How would you roughly size a battery for a 180 km EV?
Estimate energy needed at the wheels (say 44 kWh for 180 km) and divide by ~70% motor and transmission efficiency, giving about 63 kWh. Then divide by the battery's specific energy. Lead-acid (35 Wh/kg) would weigh ~1257 kg; NiMH ~733 kg; zinc-air ~191 kg.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.12.6 Approximate Battery Sizing · Page: 97
"if a lead acid battery is used (specific energy 35 Wh kg−1) the battery mass will be 1257 kg, if an NiMH battery (specific energy 60 Wh kg−1) is used the battery mass will be 733 kg"
Medium w-batt-050
Why is the projected 350 Wh/kg Li-ion specific energy by 2020 a big deal?
An Economist report cited by the textbook predicted lithium batteries could reach 350 Wh/kg by 2020 — about 2.5 times the cells in the Nissan Leaf (140 Wh/kg). At that point, EV range and energy density move within striking distance of internal combustion vehicles.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.13 In Conclusion · Page: 97
"A report in The Economist (6 March 2008) predicts that new lithium technology will enable batteries to have specific energies of 350 Wh kg−1 as early as 2020. This will give a battery 2.5 times the specific energy of the current battery such as that used in the Nissan Leaf."
Basic w-chg-001
How does grid electricity reach a home from a power station?
Electricity leaves the power station as AC, gets stepped up in voltage by a transformer, travels long distances along high-voltage transmission lines, then is stepped down again by another transformer near homes and businesses before reaching the customer's wall socket.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.1 Normal Existing Domestic and Industrial Electricity Supply · Page: 97
"Once electricity leaves the power station as AC electricity its voltage is stepped up by a transformer and it is then transmitted by high-voltage AC transmission lines."
Basic w-chg-002
What is the typical efficiency of grid electricity transmission?
Normal grid transmission delivers more than 90% of the power put in to the customer. Typical losses in the USA are about 6–8%. So roughly 6–8 units in every 100 are lost in wires, transformers and distribution between power station and your wall socket.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.1 Normal Existing Domestic and Industrial Electricity Supply · Page: 97
"Normally the efficiency of transmission (power transmitted to customer/power fed into network) is better than 0.9. Typical losses in the USA are believed to be 6–8%."
Basic w-chg-003
Why are very high voltages used for long-distance transmission?
High-voltage lines, with voltages up to 150,000 V, cut transmission losses sharply. Losses can be kept as low as 3% per 1000 km. Higher voltages mean lower currents for the same power, and lower currents waste less energy as heat in the transmission wires.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.1 Normal Existing Domestic and Industrial Electricity Supply · Page: 97
"High-efficiency, low-loss electricity transmission is possible using high-voltage transmission lines with voltages of up to 150 000 V. Energy losses can be kept as low as 3% per 1000 km."
Basic w-chg-004
What is an electricity 'supergrid'?
A supergrid is a network of high-voltage transmission lines that lets electricity travel very long distances with low losses. Supergrids open up alternative energy options — for example, solar power generated in North Africa could be sent to Britain, or wind power moved between windy and calm regions.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.1 Normal Existing Domestic and Industrial Electricity Supply · Page: 97
"High-voltage transmission lines allow the creation of supergrids in which energy can be transmitted for long distances."
Basic w-chg-005
How much power does a normal home electrical socket supply?
Most domestic and workplace sockets in the developed world deliver up to about 3 kW of power. That is enough to slowly recharge an EV overnight or during a workday, but too slow for fast charging — those need much higher-power sockets and special cabling.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.2 Infrastructure Needed for Charging Electric Vehicles · Page: 97
"Most buildings in the developed world have electrical sockets available which will typically supply up to 3 kW of power and hence the assumption is that EVs can be charged overnight"
Basic w-chg-006
How much power is needed to fast-charge a Nissan Leaf to 80%?
A Nissan Leaf has a 24 kWh battery. To recharge 80% of its capacity in about 48 minutes, a charging point must deliver around 24 kW of power. That is roughly eight times what a normal 3 kW home socket can supply.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.2 Infrastructure Needed for Charging Electric Vehicles · Page: 97
"The Nissan Leaf, for example, has a battery capacity of 24 kWh and if this is to recharge 80% of its capacity in 48 minutes then 24 kW of power will need to be available at the socket."
Basic w-chg-007
What infrastructure would a motorway service station need for EVs?
If 100 EVs an hour wanted to rapid-charge at a service station, each pulling about 24 kW, the site would need around 2.4 MW of installed capacity. So large-scale rapid charging requires careful planning of new grid connections, transformers and physical layout.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.2 Infrastructure Needed for Charging Electric Vehicles · Page: 98
"A hundred charging points each supplying 24 kW will create a demand for 2.4 MW. Clearly, considerable planning needs to be done on the likely infrastructure if battery EVs become common."
Basic w-chg-008
How do electric trains pick up power from supply rails?
Electric trains take current from a third rail or overhead line through steel shoes or a pantograph that slides along as the train moves. The shoes are mounted on insulators so the rail or wire is electrically separate from the running track that carries the wheels.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.3 Electricity Supply Rails · Page: 98
"The current is collected from steel shoes which are in contact with the supply rail running parallel to the track."
Basic w-chg-009
What is the voltage on the London Underground supply rails?
London Underground uses a four-rail DC supply. The rail next to the track runs at +420 V, while a central rail runs at +210 V. The current returns through the fourth rail rather than through the normal running rails carrying the train's wheels.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.3 Electricity Supply Rails · Page: 98
"London Underground trains run on DC supply rails: the rail besides the track runs at 420 V and the central rail runs at 210 V."
Basic w-chg-010
What voltage does a French TGV high-speed train use?
The French TGV high-speed train uses an overhead supply line at 25,000 V AC, 50 Hz. The 700-Series Shinkansen in Japan uses a similar 25 kV system at 60 Hz. The pantograph on the train roof slides along the wire to pick up power as the train runs.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.3 Electricity Supply Rails · Page: 98
"the overhead system for the French TGV is electrified at 25 kV, 50 Hz. The 700 Series Shinkansen is supplied with electricity at 25 kV, 60 Hz from a pantograph."
Basic w-chg-011
Why are voltages above 1 kV usually only on overhead wires?
For safety, high voltages above about 1 kV are normally limited to overhead wiring well above ground rather than to a rail along the track that people might touch. Lower voltages such as 600 V or 750 V are common with third- or fourth-rail systems instead.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.3 Electricity Supply Rails · Page: 98
"voltages above 1 kV are normally limited to overhead wiring for safety reasons."
Basic w-chg-012
What is inductive power transfer (IPT)?
Inductive power transfer is a wireless way to supply electrical energy to a vehicle. It uses the same principle as a transformer: an alternating current in a primary coil in the road induces current in a secondary pickup coil on the vehicle, with no mechanical contact needed.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.4 Inductive Power Transfer for Moving Vehicles · Page: 99
"IPT is a contactless power supply system that would allow electrical energy to be safely supplied to vehicles without any mechanical contact. IPT works by the same principle as a transformer."
Basic w-chg-013
How does IPT cope with a large gap between road and vehicle?
A normal transformer needs a tiny gap between primary and secondary. IPT compensates for a much larger air gap (over 100 mm) by raising the operating frequency to about 15,000 Hz. The system then becomes insensitive to small positional shifts of the pickup over the road track.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.4 Inductive Power Transfer for Moving Vehicles · Page: 99
"With IPT the air gap is large but the operating frequency is raised to 15 000 Hz to compensate. With the large air gap the system becomes insensitive to positional tolerances of the pickup on the track."
Basic w-chg-014
How efficient is IPT power transfer?
The track power supply and vehicle pickup of an IPT system can work with an efficiency of up to 96%. Both sides of the system are tuned (resonant), which minimises losses and unwanted harmonics. This makes IPT comparable in efficiency to a plug-in cable.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.4 Inductive Power Transfer for Moving Vehicles · Page: 100
"Efficiency: The track power supply and vehicle pickup work with an efficiency of up to 96%."
Basic w-chg-015
What range of power can IPT systems transfer?
Inductive power transfer systems can transfer large amounts of power. The book lists power ranges from about 30 kW up to 1000 kW (1 MW), so IPT is capable of supplying not just cars but also buses, monorails and trams continuously along a track.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.4 Inductive Power Transfer for Moving Vehicles · Page: 100
"Power: Large amounts of power may be transferred. Power ranges of 30–1000 kW are possible."
Basic w-chg-016
Why is IPT considered low-maintenance and safe?
IPT has no brushes, sliding contacts or other moving parts, so it is virtually maintenance-free. All components are fully enclosed and insulated, making the system touch-proof. It also produces no carbon dust or sparks, so it is suitable for sensitive or hazardous environments.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.4 Inductive Power Transfer for Moving Vehicles · Page: 100
"Maintenance: No brush wear or moving parts ensure that the IPT system is virtually maintenance free... Safety: All components are fully enclosed and insulated. Hence the system is fully touch-proof."
Basic w-chg-017
What is battery swapping?
Battery swapping is recharging an EV by physically removing its battery and replacing it with a fully charged one stored at the station. Spent batteries are then recharged in a warehouse for reuse. Denmark has a station that can swap a 250 kg lithium-ion pack in around five minutes.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.5 Battery Swapping · Page: 101
"Denmark has recently introduced a battery swapping station in which lithium ion batteries weighing around 250 kg can be replaced in specially designed cars in around 5 minutes."
Basic w-chg-018
Why is battery swapping particularly suited to taxi fleets?
A taxi fleet has predictable usage patterns and a known number of vehicles, so battery swap logistics are simpler to manage. With battery swapping, an electric taxi can be kept running continuously throughout a 24-hour day, without long charging stops to wait through.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.5 Battery Swapping · Page: 101
"The logistics of the scheme may prove slightly complex and it is perhaps better suited to taxis where the number and usage patterns can be predicted. It would allow an electric taxi to be used continuously throughout a 24 hour period."
Basic w-chg-019
What problems would mass battery swapping for cars need to solve?
Battery swapping for cars would need lots of space for storing and charging batteries, vehicles designed so packs can be removed quickly, and tight quality control so a swapped battery actually holds enough charge. Better future batteries would also reduce how often swaps are needed.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.5 Battery Swapping · Page: 102
"there would need to be considerable space devoted to storing and charging the batteries. Secondly, vehicles would need to be designed to allow batteries to be swapped quickly and easily. Thirdly, quality control on the batteries would be needed"
Basic w-chg-020
What is the difference between AC and DC railway supply?
Railway supply can be either AC or DC. Lower voltages (600 V, 750 V) tend to use DC and third or fourth rails along the track. Voltages above 1 kV usually use overhead wires and may be AC (like the TGV's 25 kV, 50 Hz) or DC (like 1500 V, 3 kV).
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.3 Electricity Supply Rails · Page: 98
"The lower voltages are often used with third- or fourth-rail systems, whereas voltages above 1 kV are normally limited to overhead wiring for safety reasons. ... Overhead systems are used with either DC or AC supply."
Indirect w-chg-021
Why does high-voltage DC transmission make sense only for long routes?
HVDC transmission needs expensive static inverters at each end to convert between AC and DC. These add capital cost and energy losses. Over long distances the savings in line losses outweigh the converter losses, but on short links those fixed costs aren't justified.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.1 Normal Existing Domestic and Industrial Electricity Supply · Page: 97
"High-voltage DC technology is only really suited to long-range transmission due to the static inverters that must be used to convert the energy to direct current for transmission. These are expensive devices"
Indirect w-chg-022
Why would a supergrid help renewable energy in Europe?
Wind and solar power are intermittent and not always near demand. A supergrid lets electricity from sunny North Africa or a windy region be sent thousands of kilometres at low loss to cloudy Britain or calm Germany — balancing renewable supply across regions and seasons.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.1 Normal Existing Domestic and Industrial Electricity Supply · Page: 97
"they allow for solar energy to be created in North Africa and transmitted with low losses to Britain or for electricity from wind energy to be transmitted from areas of high wind to areas of no wind."
Indirect w-chg-023
Why might IPT be especially useful for taxis at a rank?
Inductive pickups can be buried in the road or in a station platform. A taxi waiting at a rank could top up its battery wirelessly without any cables or driver intervention. Lateral tolerance is generous, so the taxi doesn't need to be parked precisely.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.4 Inductive Power Transfer for Moving Vehicles · Page: 101
"The track conductors may be buried in the roadway or in the charging station platform and a flat pickup is used as an energy collector. The flat construction allows large lateral tolerances."
Indirect w-chg-024
Why is standardisation of public chargers a problem?
Many EVs share public charging spots in car parks. If each car needs a different connector, voltage or protocol, drivers may not be able to safely connect to all units. The California Air Resources Board (CARB) has published guidelines aimed at standardising these car-to-charger connections.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.9.1 Battery Chargers · Page: 78
"A major problem is that of standardisation, making sure that all EVs can safely connect to all such units. The Californian Air Resources Board (CARB), which regulates such matters there, has produced guidelines"
Indirect w-chg-025
Why is most charging energy ultimately AC rather than DC?
Except for solar PV panels, almost all energy used to recharge a battery comes from an AC mains source. A charger must rectify and smooth this AC into DC before feeding the battery. Producing very smooth high-current DC is what makes large fast chargers expensive.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.9.1 Battery Chargers · Page: 76
"Except in the case of photoelectric panels, the energy for recharging a battery will nearly always come from an alternating current (AC) source such as the mains. This will need to be rectified to direct current (DC) for charging the battery."
Indirect w-chg-026
Why must rectified DC be smoothed before feeding a battery?
Without smoothing, the rectified DC has ripple. At the dips the voltage falls below the battery's terminal voltage, so no charging happens, while at the peaks it can be high enough to damage the battery. Smoothing keeps voltage in the safe operating band continuously.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.9.1 Battery Chargers · Page: 76
"The rectified direct current must have very little ripple – it must be very well 'smoothed'. This is because, at the times when the variation of the DC voltage goes below the battery voltage, no charging will take place, and at the 'high point' of the ripple it is possible that the voltage could be high enough to damage the battery."
Indirect w-chg-027
Why is opportunistic charging risky if not done carefully?
Opportunistic charging adds small amounts of charge often. Over time, cells with slightly higher self-discharge fall behind and never get fully charged, eventually failing. So the textbook says users must occasionally give the battery a full, long charge to equalise all cells to 100%.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.9.2 Charge Equalisation · Page: 79
"Some users are able to put a small amount of charge back into a battery, for example when parked in a location by a charger for a short time. This is helpful, but the user must make certain that fairly frequently a full, long charge is given to the battery"
Indirect w-chg-028
Why is keeping a hybrid's battery partly empty actually desirable?
In a hybrid, the battery must always be able to absorb regenerative braking energy. If kept fully charged, it would have nowhere to put that energy. So the battery management system keeps it partly empty but periodically tops it up to 100% to equalise the cells.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.9.2 Charge Equalisation · Page: 79
"Hybrid electric vehicles: In these it is desirable to have the battery not fully charged normally, so that it can always absorb energy from regenerative braking."
Indirect w-chg-029
Why doesn't a hybrid car need a separate plug-in charger?
On a non-rechargeable hybrid, the alternator driven by the IC engine acts as the battery's charger. The vehicle's energy-management system controls when and how much charging happens. So the hybrid never needs a wall socket — it is refuelled at a petrol station instead.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.9.1 Battery Chargers · Page: 78
"On hybrid EVs too, the charger is the alternator on the engine, and the charging will be controlled by the vehicle's energy management system."
Indirect w-chg-030
Why might IPT one day enable a 'pay-as-you-drive' road?
IPT lets vehicles draw power from buried road conductors with no contact. With electrified motorways using IPT, cars could charge while driving instead of stopping. The textbook suggests this could be applied to cars, buses and even hybrids, potentially revolutionising electric transport.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.4 Inductive Power Transfer for Moving Vehicles · Page: 101
"The use of EVs, which take power from supply rails within cities and on motorways, could itself cause a revolution in electric transport."
Indirect w-chg-031
Why is the European Commission backing HVDC links between countries?
In January 2009, the European Commission proposed €300 million to subsidise HVDC links between Ireland, Britain, the Netherlands, Germany, Denmark and Sweden. The aim is to integrate offshore wind farms and improve cross-border energy flows across Europe, supporting the rise of renewable power.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.1 Normal Existing Domestic and Industrial Electricity Supply · Page: 97
"the European Commission proposed €300 million to subsidise the development of high-voltage DC links between Ireland, Britain, the Netherlands, Germany, Denmark and Sweden, as part of a wider €1.2 billion package supporting links to offshore wind farms"
Indirect w-chg-032
Why is the Mediterranean Solar Plan interesting for EV electricity?
The Mediterranean Solar Plan aims to import large amounts of concentrating solar power into Europe from North Africa and the Middle East. If achieved, EVs charged on this supergrid would effectively be driving on solar energy, with the local emissions and well-to-wheel CO2 close to zero.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.1 Normal Existing Domestic and Industrial Electricity Supply · Page: 97
"the recently founded Union of the Mediterranean has embraced a Mediterranean Solar Plan to import large amounts of concentrating solar power into Europe from North Africa and the Middle East."
Indirect w-chg-033
Why can't existing home wiring usually do a fast EV charge?
Normal sockets supply about 3 kW. To recharge a 24 kWh Nissan Leaf to 80% in under an hour needs 24 kW — eight times more. That requires a dedicated higher-power circuit, thicker cabling, and often a new connection from the supply company at the home.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.2 Infrastructure Needed for Charging Electric Vehicles · Page: 97
"For more rapid charging, sockets which transmit greater amounts of power combined with appropriate cabling need to be available."
Indirect w-chg-034
Why might inductive charging suit car parks better than cables?
Inductive charging needs no plugging in by the driver — a coil under the parking bay does the work. There are no cables to trip on, vandalise or be left in the rain. The driver just parks, and the system handles power transfer automatically.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.2 Infrastructure Needed for Charging Electric Vehicles · Page: 98
"Inductive charging points are another option and these need to be wired in where the vehicle is parked."
Indirect w-chg-035
Why is the IPT pickup designed with a special shape?
The pickup coil has a special shape that captures the magnetic field generated by the buried track conductors as efficiently as possible. The captured AC field induces electricity in the pickup, which is then converted to DC by the regulator on board the vehicle for use.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.4 Inductive Power Transfer for Moving Vehicles · Page: 99
"The special shape of the pickup is most effective at capturing the magnetic field generated by the track conductors. The captured AC magnetic field produces electrical energy in the pickup coil"
Indirect w-chg-036
Why is multi-load operation a useful IPT feature?
Using intelligent control, multiple vehicles can each be powered independently and at the same time on a single IPT track. So one buried cable along a road can serve any number of cars or buses simultaneously, with each one drawing only the power it needs.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.4 Inductive Power Transfer for Moving Vehicles · Page: 100
"Multiple independent loads: Using intelligent control, any number of vehicles may be operated independently and simultaneously on a system."
Indirect w-chg-037
Why is IPT's unlimited operating speed important?
Mechanical pickup systems like conductor bars, festoons or cable reels have to keep contact, which limits how fast a vehicle can go before brushes spark or wear. IPT has no contact at all, so speed is not a limitation — the system works just as well at any vehicle speed.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.4 Inductive Power Transfer for Moving Vehicles · Page: 100
"Speed: With conductor bar, festoon or cable reel systems, speed is a limiting factor. With IPT the speed of operation is unlimited."
Indirect w-chg-038
Why is the Wampfler test track at Weil am Rhein significant?
The Wampfler test track at Weil am Rhein, Germany, is claimed to be the largest IPT system built. It has a total capacity of 150 kW over a 400 m track length, with six pickups each rated at 25 kW and a 120 mm air gap.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.4 Inductive Power Transfer for Moving Vehicles · Page: 100
"To date, the system is claimed to be the largest IPT system constructed, having a total capacity of 150 kW and a track length of nearly 400 m. Power is transferred to a total of six pickups on a test vehicle each having a power capability of 25 kW and an air gap of 120 mm."
Indirect w-chg-039
Why can excess power on the IPT test track be regenerated?
The test vehicle needs only about 10 kW peak, but six 25 kW pickups can supply much more. The system feeds the excess back through the conductor bar for regeneration into the mains. So energy isn't wasted — it returns to the grid, like an inverter on a solar panel.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.4 Inductive Power Transfer for Moving Vehicles · Page: 100
"Since the IPT test vehicle requires a peak power no greater than 10 kW, the excess power is returned via the conductor bar for regeneration into the mains."
Indirect w-chg-040
Why does battery swapping get easier as batteries improve?
Higher specific energy and better efficiency mean each pack carries more useful range. So drivers come for swaps less often, the storage warehouse doesn't need to be as huge, and quality control is simpler. Better batteries shrink the logistical headache of running a swap network.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.5 Battery Swapping · Page: 102
"As future batteries are developed with greater specific energy and energy efficiencies the scheme would be easier to manage as recharging would be less frequent and the storage warehouse would not need to be so large."
Medium w-chg-041
Roughly how much grid capacity does mass EV fast charging need?
If 100 cars per hour each demand a 24 kW rapid charge at a single service area, that's a 2.4 MW load. Multiplied across thousands of service stations, that's gigawatts of new grid capacity, transformers and high-voltage lines — comparable to adding several new power stations.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.2 Infrastructure Needed for Charging Electric Vehicles · Page: 98
"A hundred charging points each supplying 24 kW will create a demand for 2.4 MW."
Medium w-chg-042
How does IPT achieve high efficiency despite a 100 mm air gap?
IPT runs at 15,000 Hz (vs. the 50/60 Hz of a normal transformer) and uses resonant tuning on both the primary and secondary side. The high frequency couples energy efficiently across the large air gap, while resonance minimises losses and harmonics, pushing efficiency up to 96%.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.4 Inductive Power Transfer for Moving Vehicles · Page: 100
"Both track and pickup systems are resonant so that losses and harmonics are minimised."
Medium w-chg-043
Why is rectifier and smoothing cost a big factor in fast chargers?
The higher the DC charging current, the harder it is for rectifiers and smoothing circuits to deliver clean DC. So high-current chargers need expensive electronics. The General Motors EV1's charger, for example, cost about $2,000 in 1996 — a major slice of an early EV's bill.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.9.1 Battery Chargers · Page: 76
"the higher the direct current, the harder it is for rectifiers to produce a smooth DC output, which means that the rectifying and smoothing circuits of battery chargers are often quite expensive, especially for high-current chargers."
Medium w-chg-044
Why do many EVs use multiple-step charging for lead-acid?
Multiple-step charging raises the battery voltage to a preset upper level, then briefly removes the charge so the cell relaxes back to a lower preset level, then reapplies current. This avoids overheating and damage from sustained high voltage and is well supported by good commercial chargers.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 3. Batteries, Flywheels and Supercapacitors · Section: 3.3.4 Battery Charging · Page: 59
"The most commonly used technique for lead acid batteries is called multiple step charging. In this method the battery is charged until the cell voltage is raised to a predetermined level. The current is then switched off and the cell voltage is allowed to decay to another predetermined level"
Medium w-chg-045
Why are pantograph systems used at high speeds instead of third rails?
At high speeds, contact rails along the ground are impractical and unsafe due to the high voltages used. Pantographs slide along an overhead wire, where high voltages (e.g. 25 kV on the TGV and Shinkansen) can be safely kept far above passengers and lineside workers.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.3 Electricity Supply Rails · Page: 98
"voltages above 1 kV are normally limited to overhead wiring for safety reasons... The 700 Series Shinkansen is supplied with electricity at 25 kV, 60 Hz from a pantograph."
Medium w-chg-046
How does an IPT system include data alongside power?
An IPT system can pass signals and data along the same path it uses for power, with minimal additional hardware. An integrated power and data system is in development, meaning a single in-road infrastructure could provide both energy and communication links to passing vehicles.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.4 Inductive Power Transfer for Moving Vehicles · Page: 100
"Data transfer: Signal and data transfer is possible with IPT with minimal additional hardware. An integrated power and data system is currently being developed."
Medium w-chg-047
Why does the textbook expect future EV charging to mix several approaches?
No single method fits every case. Overnight charging at home covers most days. Workplace and public car park chargers handle daytime top-ups. Rapid 24+ kW chargers help on motorways. Inductive charging suits parking bays, and battery swapping works for fleets like taxis.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.2 Infrastructure Needed for Charging Electric Vehicles · Page: 97
"Charging at work will need to be catered for with appropriate sockets being available in the work car park, in public car parks or for street parking."
Medium w-chg-048
What does 'long-track' capability of IPT make possible?
IPT works along track lengths of up to several kilometres, and these long tracks can be repeated to give even longer systems. That means a buried IPT cable could electrify a whole bus route, motorway lane or industrial loop — vehicles charge continuously as they drive.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.4 Inductive Power Transfer for Moving Vehicles · Page: 100
"Long tracks: IPT works with track lengths of up to several kilometres in length, which may be repeated for even longer systems."
Medium w-chg-049
Why might IPT be safer than overhead wires for theme park rides?
IPT has fully enclosed and insulated components, no carbon dust, no sparks and no exposed wires. That makes it ideal for sensitive environments like theme parks and indoor monorails where overhead wires would be ugly, dangerous and hard to maintain near the public.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.4 Inductive Power Transfer for Moving Vehicles · Page: 100
"IPT may be used continuously to supply electrical energy along a predetermined track to people movers such as monorails, duo-rails or elevators, as well as theme park rides."
Medium w-chg-050
Why does the textbook compare battery swap warehouses to fuel depots?
Both store energy for vehicles. A petrol depot keeps fuel; a battery swap warehouse keeps charged packs. As future batteries get more energy-dense and recharge less often, swap warehouses get smaller — much like fuel-station tanks shrink if engines get more efficient.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 4. Electricity Supply · Section: 4.5 Battery Swapping · Page: 102
"there would need to be considerable space devoted to storing and charging the batteries... As future batteries are developed with greater specific energy and energy efficiencies the scheme would be easier to manage"
Basic w-fcell-001
What is a fuel cell?
A fuel cell is a battery-like device that uses hydrogen and oxygen to produce electricity directly through a chemical reaction. The basic reaction combines hydrogen with oxygen to form water and release energy as electric current, with no burning involved at the cell.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.1 Fuel Cells – A Real Option? · Page: 102
"The basic principle of the fuel cell is that it uses hydrogen fuel to produce electricity in a battery-like device... The product is thus water and energy."
Basic w-fcell-002
Why is a fuel cell vehicle called 'zero-emission'?
The types of fuel cells used in vehicles work at about 85°C, low enough that no nitrous oxides form from air. The only output is water and electricity, so the car produces no harmful exhaust. That is why a fuel-cell vehicle can be described as zero-emission.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.1 Fuel Cells – A Real Option? · Page: 102
"Because the types of fuel cells likely to be used in vehicles work at quite modest temperatures (∼85 ◦C) there is no nitrous oxide produced... A fuel cell vehicle could thus be described as 'zero emissions'"
Basic w-fcell-003
When was the hydrogen fuel cell invented?
The hydrogen fuel cell was invented around 1840. So the basic technology is nearly two centuries old, yet fuel cells have still not become a mainstream power source for vehicles. They are now expected to gradually make their mark over the next 20 to 30 years.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.1 Fuel Cells – A Real Option? · Page: 102
"The hydrogen fuel cell was invented in about 1840, but fuel cells are yet really to make their mark as a power source for EVs."
Basic w-fcell-004
What is the most important type of fuel cell for EVs?
For EVs, the most important type is the PEM (proton-exchange-membrane) fuel cell, also called PEMFC. Its electrolyte is a solid polymer in which protons can move. The PEMFC is used in all the most impressive demonstration fuel-cell vehicles built so far.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.2.2 Different Electrolytes · Page: 107
"The PEM fuel cell capitalises on the essential simplicity of the fuel cell. The electrolyte is a solid polymer, in which protons are mobile."
Basic w-fcell-005
What happens at the anode of a PEM fuel cell?
At the anode of a PEM fuel cell, hydrogen gas is split. Each H₂ molecule loses two electrons and becomes two H⁺ ions (protons). The electrons flow out through the external circuit as electric current, while the H⁺ ions travel through the polymer electrolyte to the cathode.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.2.1 Electrode Reactions · Page: 106
"At the anode of an acid electrolyte fuel cell the hydrogen gas ionises, releasing electrons and creating H+ ions (or protons)"
Basic w-fcell-006
What happens at the cathode of a PEM fuel cell?
At the cathode, oxygen molecules from the air combine with electrons coming back through the external circuit and with H⁺ ions arriving through the electrolyte. They form water, the only product of the cell. This is what makes fuel-cell vehicles so clean.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.2.1 Electrode Reactions · Page: 106
"At the cathode, oxygen reacts with electrons taken from the electrode, and H+ ions from the electrolyte, to form water"
Basic w-fcell-007
What does PEM stand for in PEM fuel cell?
PEM stands for proton-exchange membrane. The membrane is a solid polymer with mobile H⁺ ions (protons) baked into it, so it carries current as positive ions while blocking electrons. The electrons must instead travel through the external circuit, producing useful electric power.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.2.1 Electrode Reactions · Page: 106
"These materials are called 'proton exchange membranes' (PEMs), as an H+ ion is also a proton"
Basic w-fcell-008
Why does a PEM fuel cell use platinum?
PEM fuel cells run at low temperatures (about 80°C), so the reactions are naturally slow. Platinum is used as a catalyst to speed them up. Thanks to development, only minute amounts of platinum are now needed, and its cost is a small part of the cell's total price.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.2.2 Different Electrolytes · Page: 107
"Platinum is the catalyst, but developments in recent years mean that only minute amounts are used, and the cost of the platinum is a small part of the total price of a PEM fuel cell."
Basic w-fcell-009
What is a direct methanol fuel cell (DMFC)?
A direct methanol fuel cell is a PEM fuel cell that uses liquid methanol directly as fuel, without first reforming it into hydrogen. This makes refuelling easy, but DMFCs have very low power. Their main near-term use is in portable electronics, not vehicles.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.2.2 Different Electrolytes · Page: 107
"such cells are called direct methanol fuel cells (DMFC)s – 'direct' because they use the methanol as the fuel as it is, in liquid form... these cells have very low power"
Basic w-fcell-010
What is an alkaline fuel cell (AFC) historically known for?
The alkaline fuel cell powered NASA's Apollo missions and Shuttle Orbiter. It can be cheaper than a PEMFC but has lower power, and its alkaline electrolyte reacts with carbon dioxide from air, making terrestrial use difficult. A few demonstration EVs have used hybrid AFC/battery systems.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.2.2 Different Electrolytes · Page: 108
"the alkaline fuel cell (AFC) was used on the Apollo missions and on the Shuttle Orbiter."
Basic w-fcell-011
What temperature does a solid oxide fuel cell (SOFC) operate at?
Solid oxide fuel cells run in the region of 600 to 1000°C. The high temperature allows good reaction rates without expensive catalysts and even allows the cell to use gases such as natural gas directly. However, the ceramic materials and start-up requirements make SOFCs unsuitable for road cars.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.2.2 Different Electrolytes · Page: 108
"The solid oxide fuel cell (SOFC) operates in the region of 600–1000 ◦C."
Basic w-fcell-012
Why does a fuel cell electrode have a porous structure?
Fuel cell reactions take place where gas, electrolyte ions and electrons meet on the electrode surface. The larger the surface area, the more current can flow. Electrodes are made highly porous so their real surface area is much greater than their external length × width.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.2.3 Fuel Cell Electrodes · Page: 109
"Electrodes are made highly porous so that their real surface area is much greater than the normal length X width."
Basic w-fcell-013
How thin is a PEM fuel cell's membrane electrode assembly (MEA)?
Everything in a PEM fuel cell is very thin. The electrolyte itself is about 0.05–0.1 mm thick, each electrode is about 0.03 mm thick, and gas diffusion layers are 0.2–0.5 mm thick each. The whole anode/electrolyte/cathode (MEA) sandwich is around 1 mm thick.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.2.3 Fuel Cell Electrodes · Page: 111
"The electrolyte is about 0.05–0.1 mm thick, and each electrode is about 0.03 mm thick, with the gas diffusion layers each about 0.2–0.5 mm thick. The whole anode/electrolyte/cathode assembly... is thus typically about 1 mm thick"
Basic w-fcell-014
Why isn't a fuel cell limited by Carnot's law?
Internal-combustion engines are heat engines, so their efficiency is capped by the Carnot limit. Fuel cells convert chemical energy directly into electricity without going through a heat-engine cycle. As a result, they aren't constrained by Carnot and can in theory be more efficient than IC engines.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.3.1 Fuel Cell Efficiency and Efficiency Limits · Page: 111
"they are not heat engines. Their thermodynamics are different, and in particular their efficiency is potentially greater as they are not limited by the well-known 'Carnot limit'"
Basic w-fcell-015
What is the typical operating voltage of a working PEM fuel cell?
A working PEM fuel cell typically operates at an average cell voltage of about 0.65–0.70 V, even when drawing currents close to 1 A per cm² of electrode area. This corresponds to an electrical efficiency (against the HHV) of about 50% — much higher than any IC engine.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.3.3 Practical Fuel Cell Voltages · Page: 117
"A fuel cell will typically operate at an average cell voltage of about 0.65–0.70 V, even at currents approaching 1 A cm−2. This represents an efficiency of about 50% (with respect to the HHV)"
Basic w-fcell-016
Why is a fuel cell more efficient at low loads?
A fuel cell's voltage is higher at lower current. Since electrical energy is voltage × charge, higher voltage means higher efficiency. So a fuel cell is more efficient at part-load — the opposite of an IC engine, which is particularly inefficient at low power.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.3.3 Practical Fuel Cell Voltages · Page: 117
"a consequence of the higher cell voltage at lower currents is that the efficiency is higher at lower currents. This is a marked contrast to the IC engine, where the efficiency is particularly poor at low powers."
Basic w-fcell-017
What is a fuel cell 'stack'?
A stack is a number of fuel cells connected in series to produce a useful total voltage. Since a single cell only gives about 0.7 V while running, many cells (sometimes 80 or more) are stacked together to drive a motor or other load. Stacks look like solid blocks.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.4 Connecting Cells in Series – The Bipolar Plate · Page: 119
"Such a collection of fuel cells in series is known as a 'stack'."
Basic w-fcell-018
What is a bipolar plate?
A bipolar plate sits between two cells in a fuel cell stack. It makes electrical contact across the whole face of the cathode of one cell and the anode of the next cell at the same time, and also feeds oxygen and hydrogen to each side without letting them mix.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.4 Connecting Cells in Series – The Bipolar Plate · Page: 119
"These make connections all over the surface of one cathode and also the anode of the next cell (hence 'bipolar'). At the same time the bipolar plate serves as a means of feeding oxygen to the cathode and hydrogen to the anode."
Basic w-fcell-019
Why are fuel cells expensive to make?
Each stack has many bipolar plates that are complex to manufacture. Graphite is hard to machine, stainless steel can corrode, and plastics don't conduct well. Add seals, gas diffusion layers, catalyst-coated electrodes and assembly with very low fault tolerance — and unit cost stays high.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.4 Connecting Cells in Series – The Bipolar Plate · Page: 120
"no entirely satisfactory way of making these items has yet been developed... the bipolar plate makes a major contribution to the cost of a fuel cell, as well as its size and its weight."
Basic w-fcell-020
Why is water management critical in a PEM fuel cell?
PEM fuel cells produce water at the cathode. The polymer electrolyte must stay well hydrated to conduct H⁺ ions, but if too much water builds up the cell floods. So engineers must keep the membrane wet enough to work, without drowning the air-side reactions.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.5.1 Introduction to the Water Problem · Page: 122
"for the electrolyte to work properly, it must be very well hydrated."
Basic w-fcell-021
What is Nafion?
Nafion (made by Dupont) is the best-known proton exchange membrane material. It is a fluoropolymer related to PTFE (Teflon) but with sulfonated side chains. These attract water, creating hydrated regions through which H⁺ ions can move easily. It is the industry-standard PEMFC electrolyte.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.5.2 The Electrolyte of a PEMFC · Page: 123
"The most well known and well established of these is Nafion (from Dupont), which has been developed through several variants since the 1960s."
Basic w-fcell-022
Why is hydrogen storage so hard?
Hydrogen has very high specific energy (per kilogram) but very low density. So getting useful amounts into a small space needs very high pressures. It is also hard to liquefy — even at 22 K, liquid hydrogen has a density of only about 71 kg/m³.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 6. Hydrogen as a Fuel — Its Production and Storage · Section: 6.5.1 Introduction to the Problem · Page: 141
"although hydrogen has one of the highest specific energies (energy per kilogram)... its density is very low, and it has one of the lowest energy densities (energy per cubic metre). This means that to get a large mass of hydrogen into a small space very high pressures have to be used."
Basic w-fcell-023
What are the main ways to store hydrogen?
Hydrogen can be stored as a compressed gas in cylinders, as a cryogenic liquid (LH₂) at about 22 K, absorbed in a reversible metal hydride, or in carbon nanofibres. Chemical hydrogen carriers like methanol or sodium borohydride also act as an indirect storage method.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 6. Hydrogen as a Fuel — Its Production and Storage · Section: 6.5.1 Introduction to the Problem · Page: 141
"compression in gas cylinders; storage as a cryogenic liquid; storage in a metal absorber – as a reversible metal hydride; storage in carbon nanofibres."
Basic w-fcell-024
Is hydrogen more dangerous than other fuels?
Hydrogen needs careful handling — it leaks easily and is highly flammable. But all things considered, it is no more dangerous, and in some respects less dangerous, than common fuels like methane and propane, provided systems are well designed and regularly checked for leaks.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 6. Hydrogen as a Fuel — Its Production and Storage · Section: 6.5.2 Safety · Page: 141
"all things considered, hydrogen is no more dangerous, and in some respects it is rather less dangerous, than other commonly used fuels."
Basic w-fcell-025
How is hydrogen stored as compressed gas in a vehicle?
Hydrogen on vehicles is typically stored in cylinders with an aluminium inner liner wrapped in an aramide-fibre/epoxy composite, rated for about 300 bar working pressure and around 1200 bar burst pressure. The composite tears rather than shattering, giving good burst behaviour for safety.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 6. Hydrogen as a Fuel — Its Production and Storage · Section: 6.5.3 The Storage of Hydrogen as a Compressed Gas · Page: 141
"This tank is constructed with an aluminium inner liner 6 mm thick, around which is wrapped a composite of aramide fibre and epoxy resin... The burst pressure is 1200 bar, though the maximum pressure used is 300 bar."
Basic w-fcell-026
At what temperature is liquid hydrogen (LH₂) stored?
Liquid hydrogen, abbreviated LH₂, is stored at about 22 Kelvin (around minus 251°C). At that temperature its density is still only about 71 kg per cubic metre. The container is a large, strongly reinforced vacuum (Dewar) flask kept below 3 bar of pressure.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 6. Hydrogen as a Fuel — Its Production and Storage · Section: 6.5.4 Storage of Hydrogen as a Liquid · Page: 143
"The storage of hydrogen as a liquid (commonly called LH2), at about 22 K, is currently the only widely used method of storing large quantities of hydrogen."
Basic w-fcell-027
How does a reversible metal hydride store hydrogen?
Certain metal alloys (with titanium, iron, manganese, nickel, etc.) react with hydrogen at low pressure to form a metal hydride, soaking up the gas. Heating the hydride later releases the hydrogen on demand. The reaction is fully reversible — the alloy acts like a hydrogen sponge.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 6. Hydrogen as a Fuel — Its Production and Storage · Section: 6.5.5 Reversible Metal Hydride Hydrogen Stores · Page: 145
"Certain metals, particularly mixtures (alloys) of titanium, iron, manganese, nickel, chromium and others, can react with hydrogen to form a metal hydride in a very easily controlled reversible reaction."
Basic w-fcell-028
What is electrolysis of water?
Electrolysis uses electricity to split water into hydrogen and oxygen. Two inert electrodes are placed in water; hydrogen forms at the negative electrode and oxygen at the positive. If the electricity comes from nuclear or renewable sources, this produces carbon-free hydrogen.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 6. Hydrogen as a Fuel — Its Production and Storage · Section: 6.4 Energy Efficiency of Reforming · Page: 141
"An electrical power source is connected to two electrodes typically made from some inert metal such as platinum or stainless steel, which are placed in the water. In a properly designed cell, hydrogen will appear at the cathode... and oxygen will appear at the anode"
Basic w-fcell-029
What is fuel reforming?
Fuel reforming is making hydrogen from another fuel like methanol, methane or petrol. Steam reforming and partial oxidation both extract hydrogen, often using a catalyst. The textbook notes that the thermodynamic efficiency of reforming is between about 70% and 85% (LHV basis).
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 6. Hydrogen as a Fuel — Its Production and Storage · Section: 6.3.6 Energy Efficiency of Reforming · Page: 141
"The thermodynamic efficiency of reforming is between 70 and 85% (LHV basis) depending on the purity of the hydrogen product."
Basic w-fcell-030
Why is methanol a popular hydrogen carrier?
Methanol can be reformed to hydrogen at around 250°C — much lower than petrol, with far less carbon monoxide produced. It is liquid, easy to handle, and yields about 0.188 kg of hydrogen per kg of methanol via steam reforming. Many automakers have worked on methanol fuel-cell vehicles.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 6. Hydrogen as a Fuel — Its Production and Storage · Section: 6.6.2 Methanol · Page: 149
"Methanol is the 'human-made' carrier of hydrogen that is attracting the most interest among fuel cell developers."
Basic w-fcell-031
What is sodium borohydride and how is it used?
Sodium borohydride (NaBH₄) is a chemical hydrogen carrier. A solution reacts with water in the presence of a catalyst to release pure hydrogen and form sodium borate. Removing the catalyst stops the reaction, so hydrogen generation is highly controllable — useful for fuel-cell vehicles.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 6. Hydrogen as a Fuel — Its Production and Storage · Section: 6.6.4 Sodium Borohydride · Page: 152
"A good deal of interest has been shown in the use of sodium tetrahydridoborate, or sodium borohydride as it is usually called, as a chemical hydrogen carrier."
Basic w-fcell-032
What is the Necar 5?
The Necar 5, launched by DaimlerChrysler in November 2000, represents the state of the art in methanol fuel-cell vehicles. Based on the A-Class Mercedes, it uses a Ballard 75 kW stack with an onboard methanol reformer under the passenger compartment and reaches over 150 kph.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 6. Hydrogen as a Fuel — Its Production and Storage · Section: 6.6.2 Methanol · Page: 150
"In November 2000 DaimlerChrysler launched the Necar5 which, together with a Jeep Commander vehicle, represents the state of the art in methanol fuel cell vehicles."
Basic w-fcell-033
What are PEMFC bipolar plate materials made from?
Bipolar plates are typically made of graphite or stainless steel. Graphite is difficult to machine and brittle. Stainless steel can corrode in some fuel-cell types. Plastics would be easy to mould but don't conduct electricity well, so engineers are still searching for a better material.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.4 Connecting Cells in Series – The Bipolar Plate · Page: 120
"Graphite, for example, can be used, but this is difficult to work and brittle. Stainless steel can also be used, but this will corrode in some types of fuel cell."
Basic w-fcell-034
Why is hydrogen leak rate higher than other gases?
Hydrogen molecules are very small and very light. They diffuse through tiny gaps faster than any other gas — about 2.8 times faster than methane and 3.3 times faster than air. So fuel-cell systems must be designed with the lowest possible chance of leaks.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 6. Hydrogen as a Fuel — Its Production and Storage · Section: 6.5.2 Safety · Page: 141
"Hydrogen leaks 2.8 times faster than methane and 3.3 times faster than air."
Basic w-fcell-035
What are carbon nanofibres in hydrogen storage?
Carbon nanofibres are tiny tube-like carbon structures that can absorb hydrogen. A 1998 paper claimed they could store over 67% hydrogen by mass. That number has not been reproduced, but other groups have shown promising capacity, making nanofibres one to watch for the future.
