Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 6. Hydrogen as a Fuel — Its Production and Storage · Section: 6.5.6 Carbon Nanofibres · Page: 147
"The authors presented results suggesting that these materials could absorb in excess of 67% hydrogen by weight, a storage capacity far in excess of any of the others we have described so far."
Indirect w-fcell-036
Why are fuel-cell buses easier than fuel-cell cars to deploy first?
Buses refuel at one depot, so building hydrogen filling infrastructure is far simpler than for cars that fill up anywhere. Buses also run all day in cities where local emissions matter most. Many fuel-cell buses are already in service in Canada, the USA and Europe.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.1 Fuel Cells – A Real Option? · Page: 106
"the benefits are more keenly felt, with vehicles such as buses that run all day, in large cities. Such a vehicle is shown in Figure 5.2, and they have been used in several major cities in Canada, the USA and Europe."
Indirect w-fcell-037
Why might a hydrogen IC engine still compete with a fuel cell?
A hydrogen IC engine emits only a little nitrous oxide — no CO, CO₂ or hydrocarbons. It is cheaper and uses proven technology. While fuel cells can be more efficient, the cost gap, plus a hydrogen IC engine in a hybrid drivetrain, may still tip the balance. Time will tell.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.3.3 Practical Fuel Cell Voltages · Page: 117
"A hydrogen-powered IC engine in a hybrid drive train would be not far behind a fuel cell in efficiency, and the advantages of proven and available technology might tip the balance"
Indirect w-fcell-038
Why is the SOFC unsuitable for cars even though it's efficient?
Solid oxide fuel cells operate at 600–1000°C. The ceramic materials are difficult to handle, expensive to make, and need air pre-heaters and special cooling. They also start up slowly. So they suit always-on power plants better than cars, which start and stop many times a day.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.2.2 Different Electrolytes · Page: 108
"the ceramic materials that these cells are made from are difficult to handle, so they are expensive to manufacture, and there is still quite a large amount of extra equipment needed to make a full fuel cell system... they are not easy to start up."
Indirect w-fcell-039
Why must internal manifolding be used in PEM fuel cells?
Because the electrodes are porous, gas can escape from their edges. So edges are sealed with gaskets and the bipolar plates carry gas to the electrode surface through internal channels and small holes — this is internal manifolding. The result looks like a sealed solid block.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.4 Connecting Cells in Series – The Bipolar Plate · Page: 120
"a system of 'internal manifolding' is used with PEMFCs. This arrangement requires a more complex bipolar plate... The plates are made larger relative to the electrodes, and have extra channels running through the stack which feed the fuel and oxygen to the electrodes."
Indirect w-fcell-040
Why is LH₂ inherently inefficient to produce?
Liquefying hydrogen takes several stages: compress, cool to about 78 K with liquid nitrogen, then expand through a turbine, plus a special isomer-conversion step. Even in the best plants, about 25% of the hydrogen's heating value is consumed. Smaller plants consume up to 45%.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 6. Hydrogen as a Fuel — Its Production and Storage · Section: 6.5.4 Storage of Hydrogen as a Liquid · Page: 144
"the energy required to liquefy the gas under the very best of circumstances is about 25% of the specific enthalpy or heating value of the hydrogen."
Indirect w-fcell-041
Why do metal hydride stores need high-purity hydrogen?
Impurities react permanently with the metal alloy, contaminating the absorber and reducing its capacity. So the hydrogen used must be at least 99.999% pure. That is one of the main drawbacks of metal hydride storage, alongside heavy weight and slow filling.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 6. Hydrogen as a Fuel — Its Production and Storage · Section: 6.5.5 Reversible Metal Hydride Hydrogen Stores · Page: 147
"another important problem is that the containers are damaged by impurities in the hydrogen, because the metal absorbers will react permanently with them. So a high-purity hydrogen, at least 99.999% pure, must be used."
Indirect w-fcell-042
Why are metal hydride stores actually quite safe?
The hydrogen in a metal hydride is held at modest pressure (a few bar), not the hundreds of bar of a compressed cylinder. If the container is damaged, gas cools as it releases, slowing further release. So there is no sudden, dangerous discharge — a key safety advantage.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 6. Hydrogen as a Fuel — Its Production and Storage · Section: 6.5.5 Reversible Metal Hydride Hydrogen Stores · Page: 147
"The hydrogen is not stored at significant pressure, and so cannot rapidly and dangerously discharge. Indeed, if the valve is damaged or there is a leak in the system, the temperature of the container will fall, which will inhibit the release of the gas."
Indirect w-fcell-043
Why is methanol reforming cleaner than petrol reforming?
Methanol reforming happens at around 250°C, far below the temperatures needed for petrol. It also produces much less carbon monoxide, so much less clean-up is required to feed the resulting hydrogen to a PEM fuel cell. Methanol is also a liquid, simple to handle.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 6. Hydrogen as a Fuel — Its Production and Storage · Section: 6.6.2 Methanol · Page: 149
"the reaction takes place at temperatures of around 250 ◦C, which is far less than those needed for the reformation of gasoline... Also, the amount of carbon monoxide produced is far less, which means that far less chemical processing is needed to remove it."
Indirect w-fcell-044
Why is sodium borohydride limited despite its safety advantages?
Sodium borohydride is the safest liquid hydrogen carrier, gives pure hydrogen and is easy to control. But it is very expensive — about $630 per kg of hydrogen produced, more than 100× the cost of using grid electricity to electrolyse water. Production cost must fall first.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 6. Hydrogen as a Fuel — Its Production and Storage · Section: 6.6.4 Sodium Borohydride · Page: 155
"the cost of producing hydrogen this way is about $630 per kilogram. This is over 100 times more expensive than using an electrolyser driven by grid-supplied electricity."
Indirect w-fcell-045
Why is fuel cell electrode area such a vital figure of merit?
Gas, ions and electrons all have to meet on the electrode surface for current to flow. The larger the electrode area, the more of these reactions can happen per second. So fuel-cell performance is usually quoted in current per square centimetre, not just total power.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.2.3 Fuel Cell Electrodes · Page: 109
"the larger the electrode area, the more scope there is for this to happen, and the greater the current. This is very important. Indeed, electrode area is such a vital issue that the performance of a fuel cell design is often quoted in terms of the current per square centimetre."
Medium w-fcell-046
What three losses cause a fuel cell's voltage to drop with current?
Three losses act in series: activation loss (energy needed to start the cathode reaction, causes initial drop), ohmic loss (resistance of electrolyte and electrodes, follows Ohm's law), and concentration loss (oxygen runs short at high currents, voltage drops fast). Together they shape the cell's V-I curve.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.3.3 Practical Fuel Cell Voltages · Page: 117
"1. ... 'activation energy' causes a voltage drop... 2. The resistance of the electrolyte and the electrodes causes a voltage drop... 3. At very high currents, the air gets depleted of oxygen... 'mass transfer' or 'concentration' voltage loss."
Medium w-fcell-047
How is fuel cell efficiency calculated from its voltage?
If a fuel cell were 100% efficient (using HHV), each cell would produce 1.48 V. Real cells output a lower voltage V_c. Efficiency equals V_c divided by 1.48 V (or 1.25 V if using LHV). So measuring stack voltage and dividing by cell count gives instant efficiency.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.3.2 Efficiency and the Fuel Cell Voltage · Page: 115
"the '100% efficient' voltage for a single cell is 1.48 V if using the HHV or 1.25 V if using the LHV."
Medium w-fcell-048
Why is the Nernst voltage rise smaller than the real voltage rise with pressure?
The Nernst equation predicts a small voltage rise when pressure doubles. But in real low-temperature PEM cells the actual rise is nearly ten times bigger, because pressure also reduces the cathode activation voltage loss. Even so, the energy cost of pumping the air up often outweighs the gain.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.3.4 The Effect of Pressure and Gas Concentration · Page: 119
"in practice the voltage increase would typically be about 0.04 V, nearly 10 times as much. Even so, we should note that the increase is still not large, and that there is considerable energy cost in running the system at higher pressure."
Medium w-fcell-049
Why do PEMFC vehicles still run their cells above atmospheric pressure?
PEM fuel cell stacks in vehicles run at 1.5–2.0 bar, slightly above ambient. The aim isn't mainly the small voltage boost. It is that pressurising the air makes water balance in the PEM electrolyte much easier to manage — keeping the membrane wet enough without flooding the cathode.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.3.4 The Effect of Pressure and Gas Concentration · Page: 119
"it is the case that most PEMFCs in vehicle applications are run at a pressure distinctly above air pressure, typically between 1.5 and 2.0 bar... they make the water balance in the PEMFC much easier to maintain."
Medium w-fcell-050
How does Nafion's sulfonated side chain enable proton conduction?
Nafion's polymer backbone is hydrophobic (water-hating), but each sulfonated side chain ends in HSO₃, which is hydrophilic. Water clusters around these acid groups, creating dilute-acid channels through which H⁺ ions can hop. Around 20 water molecules per side chain gives a conductivity of about 0.1 S/cm.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 5. Fuel Cells · Section: 5.5.2 The Electrolyte of a PEMFC · Page: 125
"In a well-hydrated electrolyte there will be about 20 water molecules for each SO3− side chain. This will typically give a conductivity of about 0.1 S cm−1."
Basic w-mot-001
What is a brushed DC motor?
A brushed DC motor is the simplest electric motor type. It has permanent magnets (or coils) producing the magnetic field, a rotating armature carrying current via brushes and a commutator that reverses the current at the right moment to keep the rotor turning in one direction.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.1 Operation of the Basic DC Motor · Page: 162
"the simplest form of electric motor, at least to understand, is the 'brushed' DC motor. This type of motor is very widely used in applications such as portable tools, toys, electrically operated windows in cars"
Basic w-mot-002
What does the commutator do in a brushed DC motor?
The commutator is a split-ring contact on the rotor. As the rotor turns, the commutator reverses the direction of current in the armature coil at exactly the right moment. This keeps the force on the coil acting in the same direction, so the rotor keeps turning.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.1 Operation of the Basic DC Motor · Page: 162
"The commutator action ensures that the current in the coil keeps changing direction so that the force is in the same direction, even though the coil has moved."
Basic w-mot-003
What is the armature in a DC motor?
The armature is the rotating wire coil of a DC motor, wound around an iron core to strengthen the magnetic field. As current flows through it, the magnetic field exerts a force that turns the rotor and produces useful torque on the motor's output shaft.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.1 Operation of the Basic DC Motor · Page: 162
"The rotating wire coil, often called the armature, is wound round a piece of iron, so that the magnetic field of the magnets does not have to cross a large air gap"
Basic w-mot-004
What is back EMF in a DC motor?
As the motor armature turns through the magnetic field, it acts as a generator and produces its own voltage. This voltage opposes the supply voltage and is called the back EMF. The faster the motor spins, the larger the back EMF, which reduces the current flowing in.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.2 Torque Speed Characteristics · Page: 164
"The voltage generated is usually called the 'back EMF', hence the symbol Eb."
Basic w-mot-005
Why does a DC motor have maximum torque at zero speed?
At zero speed there is no back EMF, so the full supply voltage drives current through the armature resistance, giving the largest possible current and hence the largest torque. As the motor speeds up, back EMF grows, current drops and torque steadily falls with increasing rotational speed.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.2 Torque Speed Characteristics · Page: 166
"the torque from this type of motor has a maximum value at zero speed, when stalled, and it then falls steadily with increasing speed."
Basic w-mot-006
How do you control the speed of a brushed DC motor?
You can control a brushed DC motor's speed in two ways: by varying the supply voltage to the armature, or, where the magnetic field is produced by coils, by varying the field current. Lowering the supply voltage reduces maximum torque, while weakening the field gives high-speed/low-torque operation.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.3 Controlling the Brushed DC Motor · Page: 167
"the brushed DC motor is very flexible as to control method, especially if the magnetic flux can be varied."
Basic w-mot-007
What is a separately excited DC motor?
In a separately excited DC motor, the field winding (which sets the magnetic field) is fed from a separate supply, independent of the armature. So the field current and armature voltage can be controlled separately, giving great flexibility in choosing torque and speed over a wide range.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.4 Providing the Magnetic Field for DC Motors · Page: 169
"The separately excited motor of Figure 7.6c allows us to have independent control of both the magnetic flux (by controlling the voltage on the field winding Ef) and also the supply voltage Es."
Basic w-mot-008
What are the four main losses in an electric motor?
An electric motor has four main losses: copper losses (I²R heating in the windings and brushes), iron losses (hysteresis and eddy currents in the iron core), friction-and-windage losses, and a constant loss for things like field windings or control electronics that run all the time.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.5 DC Motor Efficiency · Page: 169
"The major sources of loss in the brushed DC electric motor are the same as for all types of electric motor, and can be divided into four main types."
Basic w-mot-009
What are copper losses in a motor?
Copper losses are the I²R heating losses caused by the electrical resistance of the motor's windings and brushes. They depend on the square of the current, which is itself proportional to torque. In small motors, copper losses are usually the largest source of inefficiency.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.5 DC Motor Efficiency · Page: 169
"These copper losses are probably the most straightforward to understand and, especially in smaller motors, they are the largest cause of inefficiency."
Basic w-mot-010
What are iron losses?
Iron losses come from the iron parts of the rotor experiencing a constantly changing magnetic field. Two types occur: hysteresis (energy needed to repeatedly re-magnetise the iron) and eddy currents (small circulating currents induced in the iron). Both losses grow with speed and waste energy as heat.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.5 DC Motor Efficiency · Page: 169
"The second major source of losses is the iron losses, because they are caused by magnetic effects in the iron of the motor"
Basic w-mot-011
Why are motor rotors made from thin laminated sheets?
Eddy currents flow in loops within the iron rotor, heating it and wasting energy. Building the rotor from many thin iron sheets, each separated by a layer of paint, breaks up these loops by raising the iron's effective electrical resistance, greatly reducing eddy-current losses.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.5 DC Motor Efficiency · Page: 169
"These eddy currents are minimised by making the iron rotor not out of one piece, but using thin sheets all bolted or glued together. Each sheet is separated from its neighbour by a layer of paint."
Basic w-mot-012
Why can a motor be driven over its rated power for short periods?
A motor's rated power is the level it can run at all day without overheating. Because the motor takes time to warm up, it can briefly handle higher power if it's not already hot. EVs exploit this during short bursts of acceleration when extra power is needed.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.6 Motor Losses and Motor Size · Page: 172
"if it is at, say, 50 ◦C, we can run it in excess of 5 kW, and its temperature will begin to increase quite rapidly. However, if we do not do this for more than about min, then the temperature will not have time to rise to a dangerous value."
Basic w-mot-013
What is dynamic braking?
Dynamic braking slows a motor by switching it from the battery to a load resistor. The motor's kinetic energy is converted back to electricity, which is then dissipated as heat in the resistor. You control braking torque by choosing the resistor value — no energy is recovered, though.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.7 Electric Motors as Brakes · Page: 173
"This way of slowing down an electric motor, using a resistor, is known as 'dynamic braking'."
Basic w-mot-014
What is regenerative braking electrically?
In regenerative braking, the motor is connected to a battery or capacitor instead of a resistor. Kinetic energy is converted to electricity by the motor and stored in the battery for reuse, slowing the vehicle. A DC/DC converter is normally needed to match motor voltage to battery voltage.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.7 Electric Motors as Brakes · Page: 173
"If the resistor of Figure 7.8 were replaced by a battery, then we would have a system known as 'regenerative braking'."
Basic w-mot-015
What is a MOSFET and where is it used in EV electronics?
A MOSFET (metal-oxide-semiconductor field-effect transistor) is an electronic switch turned on by a small gate voltage. It has a very low on-state resistance (sometimes 0.01 Ω) for low-voltage devices and is widely used in EV power electronic systems below about 1 kW.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.2.1 Switching Devices · Page: 176
"MOSFETs are widely used in low-voltage systems of power less than about 1 kW."
Basic w-mot-016
What is an IGBT?
An IGBT (insulated gate bipolar transistor) combines a bipolar transistor with a MOSFET, taking advantages of both. It needs only a low gate voltage to switch and handles higher voltages and currents than MOSFETs. IGBTs are now the universal switch from about 1 kW up to hundreds of kilowatts.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.2.1 Switching Devices · Page: 176
"the IGBT is now almost universally the electronic switch of choice in systems from 1 kW up to several hundred kilowatts"
Basic w-mot-017
What is a step-down (buck) converter?
A step-down or buck converter is a switching circuit that produces a lower output voltage from a higher input voltage. Using an electronic switch, an inductor and a diode, it can chop the input on and off so the average output voltage is whatever you choose.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.2.2 Step-Down or 'Buck' Regulators · Page: 177
"The 'step-down' or 'buck' switching regulator (or chopper)... The essential components are an electronic switch with an associated drive circuit, a diode and an inductor."
Basic w-mot-018
What efficiency can a step-down chopper achieve?
A typical step-down chopper circuit should reach more than 90% efficiency. In higher-voltage systems of around 100 V or more, even higher efficiencies — as much as 98% — are possible. This makes the buck regulator a very efficient way to control motor voltage.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.2.2 Step-Down or 'Buck' Regulators · Page: 178
"The efficiency of such a step-down chopper circuit should be over 90%. In higher voltage systems, about 100 V or more, efficiencies as high as 98% are possible."
Basic w-mot-019
What is a step-up (boost) converter?
A step-up or boost converter raises a lower DC voltage to a higher one. It stores energy briefly in an inductor while the switch is on, then releases it through a diode to charge a capacitor at a higher voltage. Essential for regenerative braking into a battery.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.2.3 Step-Up or Boost' Switching Regulator · Page: 178
"It is often desirable to step-up or boost a DC voltage, regenerative braking being just one example. This can also be done quite simply and efficiently using switching circuits."
Basic w-mot-020
What is an inverter?
An inverter is a circuit that converts DC from a battery or fuel cell into AC needed by motors like the induction motor or brushless DC motor. It uses four or six electronic switches in an H-bridge or three-phase configuration to chop the DC into alternating waveforms.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.2.4 Single-Phase Inverters · Page: 181
"The circuit that produces AC from DC sources such as batteries and fuel cells is known as an inverter."
Basic w-mot-021
What is pulse-width modulation (PWM)?
Pulse-width modulation is a technique where an inverter switches its electronic switches on and off many times per AC cycle, varying the on-time of each pulse. The result is an output current that more closely resembles a sine wave, with weaker harmonics that cause less motor heating.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.2.4 Single-Phase Inverters · Page: 183
"This method is known as 'pulse width modulation'"
Basic w-mot-022
What is a three-phase AC supply?
A three-phase supply has three alternating voltages, equal in size but 120° out of phase. Most large EV motors use three coils fed by three-phase AC. A three-phase inverter uses six electronic switches with freewheeling diodes to generate the three phases from a DC source.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.2.5 Three Phase · Page: 183
"Most large motors, of the type used in electric vehicles, have three sets of coils rather than just one. For these systems – as well as for regular mains systems – 'three-phase' AC supply is needed."
Basic w-mot-023
What is a brushless DC (BLDC) motor?
A BLDC motor is really an AC motor that takes its alternating current from an inverter fed by a DC supply. Its rotor is a permanent magnet, and electronics switch the stator coils to keep the rotor turning. There are no brushes — they're replaced by electronic commutation.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.3.2 The Brushless DC Motor · Page: 185
"The brushless DC (BLDC) motor is really an AC motor! The current through it alternates"
Basic w-mot-024
How does a BLDC motor know when to switch its coils?
The current switching must be synchronised with rotor position. BLDC motors use rotor-position sensors — usually Hall-effect sensors that detect the rotor magnets, or optical sensors. The controller microprocessor reads these signals and switches the coil currents at exactly the right moment.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.3.2 The Brushless DC Motor · Page: 186
"the switching of the current must be synchronised with the position of the rotor. This is done using sensors. These are often Hall effect sensors that use the magnetism of the rotor to sense its position"
Basic w-mot-025
What is a switched reluctance motor (SRM)?
An SRM has a simple iron rotor with no magnets or coils. The stator carries coils. When a coil is energised, the rotor is pulled into alignment with the magnetic field, minimising the air gap. Switching coils in sequence turns the rotor smoothly with simple, rugged construction.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.3.3 Switched Reluctance Motors · Page: 189
"the iron stator and rotor are magnetised by a current through the coil on the stator. Because the rotor is out of line with the magnetic field a torque will be produced to minimise the air gap"
Basic w-mot-026
Why is the SRM rotor so simple?
In an SRM the rotor is just a piece of magnetically soft iron with shaped 'salient poles' — there are no windings, brushes or permanent magnets on it. The rotor is therefore simple, cheap, rugged and can run at high speeds without flux from rotor magnets limiting the design.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.3.3 Switched Reluctance Motors · Page: 190
"In the SRM, the rotor is simply a piece of magnetically soft iron. Also, the current in the coil does not need to alternate. Essentially then, this is a very simple and potentially low-cost motor."
Basic w-mot-027
What is an induction motor?
An induction motor has three-phase coils on the stator producing a rotating magnetic field, and a 'cage' of copper or aluminium bars as the rotor. The rotating field induces currents in the bars, which then experience a force that drags the rotor along after the field.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.3.4 The Induction Motor · Page: 193
"The rotor usually consists of copper or aluminium rods, all electrically linked (short-circuited) at the end, forming a kind of cage"
Basic w-mot-028
What is 'slip' in an induction motor?
Slip is the difference between the speed of the stator's rotating magnetic field and the speed of the rotor. The rotor must run slightly slower than the field, so that field lines keep cutting the rotor bars and inducing current. If they matched, no torque would be produced.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.3.4 The Induction Motor · Page: 193
"If the rotor were to go at the same speed as the magnetic field, there would be no relative velocity between the rotating field and the conductors, and so no induced current and no torque."
Basic w-mot-029
How is induction motor speed controlled in an EV?
Induction motor speed is controlled by varying the frequency of the three-phase supply from the inverter. A higher frequency means a faster rotating magnetic field and a faster rotor. Speed feedback adjusts frequency to keep the actual speed close to the desired value despite slip variations.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.3.4 The Induction Motor · Page: 195
"A much better way is to control the frequency of the three-phase supply. Using a circuit such as that of Figure 7.21 this is easily done."
Basic w-mot-030
Why are induction motors so widely used despite being slightly less efficient?
Induction motors are produced in very high volumes, making them reasonably priced. Their technology is highly mature, very reliable, and uses cheap, well-developed materials. The 1–2% efficiency drop versus brushless types is offset by ruggedness, simple rotor (no magnets) and proven long service life.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.3.4 The Induction Motor · Page: 195
"Induction motors are very widely used. Very high volume of production makes for a very reasonably priced product. Much research has gone into developing the best possible materials."
Basic w-mot-031
What is an efficiency map for a motor?
An efficiency map is a chart that plots motor efficiency at every combination of torque and speed. It shows where the motor performs best — typically a narrow sweet spot — and where efficiency drops. Engineers use it to choose a motor matched to the vehicle's typical operating conditions.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.5 DC Motor Efficiency · Page: 172
"It is useful to plot the values of efficiency on a torque/speed graph, giving what is sometimes known as an 'efficiency map' for the motor"
Basic w-mot-032
Why do larger motors tend to be more efficient?
Larger motors have better surface-area-to-volume ratios and can dissipate heat more easily. Their relative copper, iron and friction losses are smaller as a fraction of total power. Class-1 efficiency standards bear this out for induction motors, and the same trend applies to BLDC and SRM types.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.4.1 Improving Motor Efficiency · Page: 195
"The first general point is that motors become more efficient as their size increases."
Basic w-mot-033
Why are higher-speed motors usually more efficient?
Many motor losses are proportional to torque, not power. For the same power, a higher-speed motor produces less torque and so has lower copper losses. That's why EV drivetrains often pair a high-speed motor with a 10:1 reduction gearbox rather than a direct-drive low-speed motor.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.4.1 Improving Motor Efficiency · Page: 195
"Higher speed motors are more efficient than lower ones. The reason for this is that one of the most important losses in a motor is proportional to torque, rather than power"
Basic w-mot-034
Why does motor mass depend more on torque than power?
Motor size is set mostly by the torque it must produce, not its peak power. So a lighter, smaller motor can deliver the same power at higher speed and lower torque. EVs typically use a high-speed motor with a gearbox to save weight and space versus a slow direct-drive motor.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.4.2 Motor Mass · Page: 198
"The size of the motor is most strongly influenced by the motor torque, rather than power. The consequence is that a higher speed, lower torque motor will be smaller."
Basic w-mot-035
What is an 'inside-out' motor in hybrid vehicles?
An inside-out motor reverses the layout: the stationary coils sit on the inside, while the rotor — a band of permanent magnets — turns on the outside. It's a flat, large-diameter design that fits on the engine crankshaft. The centrifugal force keeps the magnets held against the rotor.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.5 Electric Machines for Hybrid Vehicles · Page: 199
"they are usually 'turned inside out', with the stationary coils being on the inside, and the rotor being a band of magnets moving outside the coil... centrifugal force on the magnets tends to make them stay in place"
Indirect w-mot-036
Why is the brushed DC motor at a fundamental size disadvantage?
In a brushed DC motor virtually all losses happen in the rotor at the centre, where heat is hard to remove. In brushless designs most losses happen on the outer stator, which can be cooled by air or liquid easily, so brushless motors can be smaller.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.6 Motor Losses and Motor Size · Page: 172
"In this type of motor virtually all the losses occur in the rotor at the centre of the motor. This means that the heat generated is much more difficult to remove."
Indirect w-mot-037
Why does a DC/DC converter make regenerative braking possible?
When braking, the motor often generates a voltage lower than the battery's. Without a converter, no current would flow into the battery, so no energy would be recovered. A boost DC/DC converter raises the motor's low voltage to the battery's higher voltage, letting energy flow back in.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.7 Electric Motors as Brakes · Page: 174
"the motor voltage might be considerably lower than the battery voltage, but it can still be providing charge to the battery."
Indirect w-mot-038
Why are linear voltage regulators banned in EV traction?
A linear regulator drops voltage by turning surplus electrical power into heat. In a battery EV, that wastes precious energy. Linear regulators have no place in systems where efficiency is paramount, which is why switching choppers (buck/boost) are used to control motor voltage in electric vehicles.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.2.2 Step-Down or 'Buck' Regulators · Page: 178
"Linear regulators have no place in systems where efficiency is paramount, such as an electric vehicle."
Indirect w-mot-039
Why does PWM produce less motor heating than simple square-wave inverters?
Simple square-wave AC contains many high-frequency harmonics, which speed up iron losses in the motor. PWM uses many short pulses per cycle, giving a current closer to a sine wave with weaker harmonics. Less harmonic content means less iron loss and less heating in the motor.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.2.4 Single-Phase Inverters · Page: 183
"With motors the main problem is that the harmonics can increase the iron losses... For this reason the simple switching pattern just described is often not used in favour of a more complex system"
Indirect w-mot-040
Why is the BLDC motor sometimes called a permanent-magnet synchronous motor?
Its rotor is a permanent magnet, and the inverter feeds AC into the stator coils. The rotor turns in synchronism with the supply frequency, just like a synchronous AC motor. That's why the BLDC is also called a self-synchronous AC motor, variable-frequency synchronous motor or electronically commutated motor.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.3.2 The Brushless DC Motor · Page: 185
"it is also known as a 'self-synchronous AC motor', a 'variable frequency synchronous motor', a 'permanent magnet synchronous motor' and an 'electronically commutated motor'"
Indirect w-mot-041
Why are SRMs sometimes called noisy compared to other motors?
Each SRM coil is pulsed on and off at exactly the right moment. Small errors in timing cause torque ripple, producing a 'thumping' acoustic noise. With more coils and digital signal processors to do precise timing, modern SRMs are much smoother and quieter than early designs.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.3.3 Switched Reluctance Motors · Page: 191
"So, the torque is much more variable, and as a result early SRMs had a reputation for being noisy."
Indirect w-mot-042
Why are SRMs especially good across a wide range of speed and torque?
Although peak efficiency of an SRM may be a little below the BLDC motor's, SRMs hold their efficiency over a much wider range of speed and torque than other motor types. That makes them well suited to vehicles, which spend a lot of time away from any single 'sweet spot'.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.3.3 Switched Reluctance Motors · Page: 192
"Although the peak efficiency of the SRM may be slightly below that of the BLDC motor, SRMs maintain their efficiency over a wider range of speed and torque than any other motor type."
Indirect w-mot-043
Why are PM synchronous motors replacing induction motors in many EVs?
Permanent-magnet synchronous motors have a higher torque-per-litre ratio than induction motors, and falling magnet manufacturing costs make them economical. So they tend to give more torque from less volume and weight than induction motors, which is exactly what EV designers want to fit under the hood.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.3.2 The Brushless DC Motor · Page: 189
"permanent magnet motors have a higher torque-to-volume ratio as compared with the induction motors. Also, the decrease in the manufacturing cost of permanent magnets makes the permanent magnet motors appealing."
Indirect w-mot-044
Why might an EV use a high-speed motor with a gearbox instead of direct drive?
Motor size scales with torque. A high-speed motor with a 10:1 gearbox gives the wheel torque you need from a small, light motor. A direct-drive low-speed motor would need to be much bigger and heavier. So gearing trades a small reduction in efficiency for a major saving in motor mass.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.4.2 Motor Mass · Page: 198
"if a low-speed rotation is needed, a high-speed motor with a gearbox will be lighter and smaller than a low-speed motor."
Indirect w-mot-045
Why is liquid cooling worth the complexity in high-power EV motors?
Motor power per kilogram is set by how fast you can carry away the waste heat. Liquid cooling lifts heat out of the motor much faster than air, so the same motor frame can handle more power. It also runs cooler, lowering winding resistance and improving efficiency by about 1%.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.4.2 Motor Mass · Page: 197
"The difference between the air-cooled and liquid cooled BLDC motor is most marked. The reason for this is that the size of the motor has to be large enough to dispose of the heat losses."
Medium w-mot-046
What formula gives total motor losses combining all four loss types?
The textbook combines copper losses (kc·T²), iron+friction losses proportional to angular speed (kᵢ·ω plus windage), and a constant offset C. Total losses ≈ kc·T² + kᵢ·ω + kw·ω³ + C. Plugging this and output power into η = P_out/(P_out + losses) gives the motor's efficiency.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.5 DC Motor Efficiency · Page: 169
"it helps to combine the terms for the iron losses and the friction losses, as both are proportional to motor speed."
Medium w-mot-047
Why does a brushed DC motor's stall torque calculation produce a huge number?
At zero speed there is no back EMF, so only the small armature resistance limits current. A 24 V supply across 0.016 Ω could in theory drive 1500 A and 205 N·m of torque. A current limit (around 250–350 A for 5 s) is essential to protect the motor.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.1.2 Torque Speed Characteristics · Page: 167
"At zero speed there is no back EMF, and thus only this armature resistance Ra opposes the 24 V supply, so the current would be... This is clearly far too large a current."
Medium w-mot-048
Why is the boost regulator's efficiency a bit lower than the buck's?
In a boost converter, the inductor and switch currents are higher than the output current, and all charge passes through the diode (0.6 V drop), adding losses. So efficiency is usually a bit lower than for a buck regulator. Still, over 80% is normal — and 95%+ above 100 V.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.2.3 Step-Up or Boost' Switching Regulator · Page: 180
"the efficiency of these boost regulators is somewhat less than for the buck. Nevertheless, over 80% should normally be obtained, and in systems where the initial voltage is higher (over 100 V), efficiencies of 95% or more are possible."
Medium w-mot-049
How does a six-pole motor differ from a two-pole induction motor?
An induction motor's magnetic field can be wound to have two, four, six or more poles. The field rotates at the supply frequency divided by the number of pole pairs. So a six-pole motor turns one-third the speed of a two-pole on the same AC supply.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.3.4 The Induction Motor · Page: 193
"The speed of rotation of the magnetic field is the supply frequency divided by the number of pole pairs. So, a four-pole motor will turn at half the speed of a two-pole motor"
Medium w-mot-050
Why does an inverter limit induction motor torque at high speed?
Coil impedance rises with frequency, so at higher frequencies the same voltage drives less current. With a fixed-voltage source (battery or fuel cell) and a frequency-controlled inverter, available current — and so available torque — falls as speed rises. Maximum torque ends up roughly inversely proportional to speed.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.3.4 The Induction Motor · Page: 195
"as the frequency increases, the current reduces, if the voltage is constant, because of the inductance of the coils having an impedance that is proportional to the frequency. The result is that, if the inverter is fed from a fixed voltage, the maximum torque is inversely proportional to the speed."
Basic w-mod-001
What is 'tractive effort' in vehicle modelling?
Tractive effort is the forward force the vehicle's powertrain delivers to the road through its drive wheels. It must do four jobs at once: overcome rolling resistance, push through aerodynamic drag, climb any slope and, if speed is changing, accelerate or slow down the vehicle.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.1 Introduction · Page: 204
"The first step in vehicle performance modelling is to produce an equation for the required 'tractive effort'. This is the force propelling the vehicle forward, transmitted to the ground through the drive wheels."
Basic w-mod-002
What is rolling resistance?
Rolling resistance is the force opposing motion mainly due to hysteresis losses in the tyres, plus friction in bearings and gears. It is roughly constant with speed and is proportional to vehicle weight. Hard, well-inflated tyres have low rolling resistance and let the vehicle roll easily.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.2 Rolling Resistance Force · Page: 204
"The rolling resistance is primarily due to the hysteresis losses in the vehicle tyres. Friction in bearings and the gearing system also play their part."
Basic w-mod-003
What is the coefficient of rolling resistance μ_rr?
μ_rr is the dimensionless number that links rolling resistance force to vehicle weight: force equals μ_rr × weight. A typical radial-ply tyre has μ_rr around 0.015. Special low-resistance tyres developed for EVs can get the value down to about 0.005.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.2 Rolling Resistance Force · Page: 204
"Typical values of μrr are 0.015 for a radial ply tyre, down to about 0.005 for tyres developed especially for electric vehicles."
Basic w-mod-004
What is aerodynamic drag?
Aerodynamic drag is the force the body of the moving vehicle must push against the air. It is proportional to air density, the vehicle's frontal area, the drag coefficient and the square of the speed. Doubling speed quadruples the drag force.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.3 Aerodynamic Drag · Page: 204
"This part of the force is due to the friction of the vehicle body moving through the air."
Basic w-mod-005
What is the drag coefficient (Cd)?
Cd is a dimensionless number describing how slippery a vehicle's shape is in air. A typical saloon car has Cd around 0.3. Some EVs achieve as low as 0.19. Buses and motorcycles, with bluffer shapes, typically have Cd values closer to 0.7.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.3 Aerodynamic Drag · Page: 204
"A typical value for a saloon car is 0.3, but some electric vehicle designs have achieved values as low as 0.19."
Basic w-mod-006
What is hill-climbing force?
Hill-climbing force is the component of the vehicle's weight that acts along the slope when going up a hill. It equals the vehicle's weight times the sine of the slope angle. It opposes motion on uphills and helps motion on downhills (becoming negative).
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.4 Hill Climbing Force · Page: 204
"The force needed to drive the vehicle up a slope is the most straightforward to find. It is simply the component of the vehicle weight that acts along the slope."
Basic w-mod-007
What is linear-acceleration force?
By Newton's second law, accelerating a vehicle of mass m needs a force F = m × a. This is the linear-acceleration term in the tractive-effort equation. If the vehicle slows down, the term goes negative, meaning the wheels are doing braking work rather than driving work.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.5 Acceleration Force · Page: 204
"This force will provide the linear acceleration of the vehicle, and is given by the well-known equation derived from Newton's third law"
Basic w-mod-008
Why must rotational acceleration of the motor be considered?
The motor's rotor must speed up too, not just the whole vehicle. Even if its moment of inertia is small, the motor spins at angular speeds much higher than the wheels (gear ratio amplifies). So accelerating it takes extra force, often added approximately as a 5% mass increase.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.5 Acceleration Force · Page: 204
"we need to consider rotational acceleration as well as linear acceleration. The main issue here is the electric motor – not necessarily because of its particularly high moment of inertia, but because of the higher angular speeds."
Basic w-mod-009
What is the formula for total tractive effort?
Total tractive effort is the sum of rolling-resistance force (F_rr), aerodynamic drag (F_ad), hill-climbing force (F_hc), linear-acceleration force (F_la) and rotor-acceleration force (F_ωa). Slowing down or going downhill makes some terms negative. This sum drives all EV performance and range modelling.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.6 Total Tractive Effort · Page: 206
"The total tractive effort is the sum of all these forces"
Basic w-mod-010
Why is acceleration to 30 or 50 kph the EV equivalent of 0–60 mph?
For IC cars, 0–60 mph is the standard acceleration test. EVs don't have a single agreed measure, but the closest equivalents are the 0–30 kph and 0–50 kph times. These shorter benchmarks are often used because many electric vehicles emphasise urban performance, not all-out top speed.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.3.1 Acceleration Performance Parameters · Page: 206
"Typically the time to accelerate from standstill to 60 mph, or 30 or 50 kph, will be given. The nearest to such a standard for electric vehicles are the 0–30 and 0–50 kph times"
Basic w-mod-011
How does motor torque vary with speed in performance modelling?
At low speeds the motor delivers a constant maximum torque T_max. Above a critical angular speed ω_c the torque falls. In a brushed DC motor it falls linearly with speed. In most brushless motors it falls in such a way that the output power stays constant.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.3.1 Acceleration Performance Parameters · Page: 208
"at low speeds, the maximum torque is a constant, until the motor speed reaches a critical value ωc after which the torque falls."
Basic w-mod-012
What is a driving cycle?
A driving cycle is a fixed pattern of vehicle speed against time, intended to mimic realistic driving. It is used in tests and simulations to measure range, fuel use or emissions under more realistic conditions than steady-state running. Many cycles exist for cities, highways and combined driving.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.4.1 Driving Cycles · Page: 214
"the vehicle is driven, in reality or in simulation, through a profile of ever-changing speeds. These test cycles have been developed with some care"
Basic w-mod-013
What is the LA-4 driving cycle?
The LA-4 cycle was based on actual traffic flows in Los Angeles. It became one of the first widely used standardised driving cycles. It was later developed into the Federal Urban Driving Schedule (FUDS), a 1500-second cycle that gives each second a different target vehicle speed.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.4.1 Driving Cycles · Page: 214
"One of the most well known of the early cycles was one based on actual traffic flows in Los Angeles, and is known as the LA-4 cycle. This was then developed into the Federal Urban Driving Schedule, or FUDS"
Basic w-mod-014
What is the FUDS driving cycle?
FUDS, the Federal Urban Driving Schedule, is a US driving cycle simulating city traffic. It lasts 1500 seconds, with a different target speed for each second. There is also a simplified 360-second version (SFUDS) that keeps the same average speed and gives very similar range simulation results.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.4.1 Driving Cycles · Page: 214
"This is a cycle lasting 1500 seconds, and for each second there is a different speed... There is also a simplified version of this cycle known as SFUDS"
Basic w-mod-015
What is the ECE-15 driving cycle?
The ECE-15 driving cycle is a European urban driving cycle, useful for testing small vehicles like battery electric cars. It uses simple periods of constant acceleration and constant velocity. In EC emission tests it is combined with the Extra-Urban Driving Cycle (EUDC), which has a maximum speed of 120 kph.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.4.1 Driving Cycles · Page: 216
"Of particular note is the ECE-15 drive cycle... which is useful for testing the performance of small vehicles such as battery electric cars."
Basic w-mod-016
What tools are commonly used for EV performance modelling?
Standard mathematical software such as MATLAB® and spreadsheets like Microsoft Excel make excellent platforms for EV performance simulations. No special programming skills are required — the equations of motion can be evaluated step by step over time to predict acceleration, top speed and range.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.1 Introduction · Page: 201
"standard mathematical and spreadsheet programs such as MATLAB® and Excel make an excellent basis for these simulations."
Basic w-mod-017
What is the value of air density typically used?
Air density changes with temperature, altitude and humidity. For most EV calculations a reasonable working value is 1.25 kg/m³. Using SI units (frontal area in m², velocity in m/s) with this air density gives an aerodynamic drag force directly in newtons.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.3 Aerodynamic Drag · Page: 204
"a value of 1.25 kg m −3 is a reasonable value to use in most cases."
Basic w-mod-018
What two types of range tests are used?
There are two main types: a constant-velocity simulation, where the car runs at one steady speed (unrealistic but simple), and a driving-cycle simulation, where speed varies continuously through a realistic city or highway profile. The second is more useful but needs more complex computation.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.4.1 Driving Cycles · Page: 214
"There are two types of calculation or test that can be performed with regard to the range of a vehicle. The first, and much the simplest, is the constant velocity simulation."
Basic w-mod-019
Why does the simple constant-velocity range test still matter?
A constant-velocity range test ignores the reality of stop-start driving and traffic, but its rules are unambiguous. Every vehicle is tested the same way. So even though the absolute range numbers may be unrealistic, they give a useful basis for comparing one vehicle directly against another.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.4.1 Driving Cycles · Page: 214
"the rules for the test are clear and unambiguous, even if the simulation is unrealistic. It can be argued that they do at least give useful comparative figures."
Basic w-mod-020
What is the Federal Highway Driving Schedule (FHDS)?
FHDS is a US driving cycle used to simulate out-of-town highway driving. It is widely used but has a top speed seen as a little low for real highways. The newer US06 cycle, with a more realistic high-speed profile, is increasingly used in its place for highway tests.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.4.1 Driving Cycles · Page: 215
"Two notable examples of these are the Federal Highway Driving Schedule, or FHDS... this cycle has a rather unrealistic maximum speed for highway driving, and the newer US06 standard, is now becoming more widely used instead."
Basic w-mod-021
Why is gear-system efficiency included in the model?
Even a single-stage gear coupling is not 100% efficient. Typical values for an EV are around 95–98%. So the force at the wheels is slightly less than ideal. The model multiplies motor torque by gear efficiency η_g to give a more accurate prediction of vehicle acceleration.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.5 Acceleration Force · Page: 205
"the system will usually be very simple, the efficiency is often very high. However, it will never be 100%, and so we should refine the equation by incorporating the gear system efficiency"
Basic w-mod-022
How is the motor's torque related to the force at the wheels?
Wheel torque equals tractive force times wheel radius r. The motor torque is connected to wheel torque by the gear ratio G. So tractive force at the wheels = G × T_motor / r, where T_motor is the motor's torque output and r is the tyre's radius.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.5 Acceleration Force · Page: 204
"clearly the axle torque equals Fter, where r is the radius of the tyre and F te is the tractive effort delivered by the powertrain. If G is the gear ratio of the system connecting the motor to the axle"
Basic w-mod-023
How does motor angular speed relate to vehicle speed?
If a vehicle moves at velocity v and the tyre has radius r, the axle turns at v/r radians per second. The motor spins at G times this — that is, motor angular speed = G·v/r. So a higher gear ratio means the motor spins faster than the wheels.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.5 Acceleration Force · Page: 205
"axle angular speed equals v /r radians per second, so motor angular speed is"
Basic w-mod-024
Why is the GM EV1 a good benchmark for performance modelling?
The GM EV1 was the first modern electric car from a really large motor company. It had an ultra-low drag coefficient (0.19), low rolling resistance (0.0048) and used variable-frequency induction motors spinning up to nearly 12,000 rpm — all real published data that's useful for validating simulations.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.3.3 Modelling the Acceleration of a Small Car · Page: 212
"an ultra-low drag coefficient C d of 0.19; a very low coefficient of rolling resistance, μrr, of 0.0048; the use of variable frequency induction motors, operating at very high speed – nearly 12 000 rpm at maximum speed."
Basic w-mod-025
What does an EV acceleration simulation look like in practice?
The equations are usually solved in small time steps (e.g. 0.1 s). At each step the model computes the tractive effort, divides by mass to get acceleration, and updates velocity. Spreadsheets like Excel or scripts in MATLAB® make this very straightforward to set up and rerun with changes.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.3.2 Modelling the Acceleration of an Electric Scooter · Page: 209
"the most versatile next step is to derive a simple numerical solution, which can then easily be used in Excel or MATLAB®."
Basic w-mod-026
What is the value of a 5% mass increase shortcut?
Sometimes the motor's moment of inertia I is unknown. A simple workaround is to ignore the angular-acceleration term entirely and instead pretend the vehicle is 5% heavier in the linear-acceleration term. This crude shortcut gives reasonable accuracy without needing to look up rotor inertia values.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.5 Acceleration Force · Page: 206
"It will quite often turn out that the moment of inertia of the motor I will not be known. In such cases a reasonable approximation is simply to increase the mass by 5% in Equation (8.4), and to ignore the Fωa term."
Basic w-mod-027
Why is range modelling more complex than top-speed modelling?
Top speed is reached in a steady-state balance of forces. But range requires summing energy used over an entire driving cycle, where every second has a different speed, acceleration, motor efficiency and battery loss. Computers handle the bookkeeping easily, but the model itself is much more involved.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.4.1 Driving Cycles · Page: 214
"During these tests the vehicle speed is almost constantly changing, and thus the performance of all the other parts of the system is also highly variable, which makes the computations more complex."
Basic w-mod-028
What does 'tyre radius' affect in the model?
The tyre radius r ties motor torque to wheel torque (F = G·T/r) and motor speed to vehicle speed (ω = G·v/r). Smaller wheels give more wheel torque for the same motor torque but spin faster, so they need higher motor speeds to reach the same road speed.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.5 Acceleration Force · Page: 204
"the axle torque equals Fter, where r is the radius of the tyre"
Basic w-mod-029
What is 'critical motor speed' ω_c?
Critical motor speed ω_c is the rotational speed at which the motor's torque begins to fall. Below ω_c the motor produces a constant maximum torque T_max. Above it, the torque either drops linearly (brushed DC) or drops in proportion to 1/speed (most brushless motors) to keep power constant.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.3.1 Acceleration Performance Parameters · Page: 208
"until the motor speed reaches a critical value ωc after which the torque falls."
Basic w-mod-030
Why is performance modelled at maximum torque, not maximum power?
For acceleration figures, you want the strongest pull at every speed. For an IC car that means running at maximum power. For an electric motor it means running at maximum available torque, since torque produces the wheel force that gives the vehicle its acceleration during the run-up.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.3.1 Acceleration Performance Parameters · Page: 206
"for electric vehicles performance simulations are carried out at maximum torque."
Indirect w-mod-031
Why is aerodynamic drag the killer of high speeds?
Drag rises as the square of speed, while the power needed to overcome it rises as the cube of speed. So pushing the vehicle from 80 to 100 kph takes far more extra power than going from 30 to 50 kph. That's why streamlining matters most at speed.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.3 Aerodynamic Drag · Page: 204
"the formula for this component is... 1/2 ρ A v²"
Indirect w-mod-032
Why can EVs achieve lower Cd than IC cars?
Internal-combustion engines need large air intakes and pipes for radiators and exhausts. EVs have far less under-vehicle plumbing and can place big components like batteries more freely. That gives designers more freedom to streamline the body, so EVs can reach Cd values that IC cars struggle to match.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.3 Aerodynamic Drag · Page: 204
"There is greater opportunity for reducing C d in electric vehicle design because there is more flexibility in the location of the major components, and there is less need for cooling air ducting and under-vehicle pipework."
Indirect w-mod-033
Why do well-pumped bicycle tyres roll so easily?
Rolling resistance depends mainly on tyre hysteresis losses, which increase with tyre flex. A well-inflated tyre flexes less under load, so its hysteresis losses are smaller. Cyclists notice the resulting low μ_rr — the bike rolls freely — although the ride feels harsher with hard tyres.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.2 Rolling Resistance Force · Page: 204
"the freewheeling performance of a bicycle becomes much better if the tyres are pumped up to a high pressure, though the ride may be less comfortable."
Indirect w-mod-034
Why does the angular-acceleration term often turn out small?
For a typical 800 kg car with a 30 kW motor, the term I·G²/r² is about 40 kg — only 5% of the 800 kg vehicle mass. So the angular acceleration of the motor matters, but compared with linear acceleration of the whole car it's a small correction.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.5 Acceleration Force · Page: 206
"The I G2/r2 term in Equation (8.8) will have a value of about 40 kg in this case. In other words, the angular acceleration force given by Equation (8.8) will typically be much smaller than the linear acceleration force"
Indirect w-mod-035
Why is finite-difference numerical solution preferred in EV modelling?
Many EV motion equations don't have neat algebraic solutions. Finite differences just approximate dv/dt by (v_n − v_{n−1}) / Δt, letting you step forward in tiny time slices, computing one velocity from the previous one. This makes modelling easy to implement in Excel or MATLAB®.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.3.2 Modelling the Acceleration of an Electric Scooter · Page: 209
"The derivative of v is simply the difference between consecutive values of v divided by the time step."
Indirect w-mod-036
Why does an EV simulation get validated against real-car data?
Models are only useful if their numbers match reality. The textbook validates its method against the published 0–60 mph time of the GM EV1 (just under 9 seconds). When the simulated number matches the real one, you gain confidence in the underlying equations and constants.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.3.3 Modelling the Acceleration of a Small Car · Page: 214
"the time taken to reach 96 kph, which is 60 mph, is just under 9 seconds. Not only is this a very respectable performance, but it is also exactly the same as given in the official figures for the performance of the real vehicle."
Indirect w-mod-037
Why might driving cycles give very different range from the constant-speed test?
City driving cycles include lots of acceleration, deceleration and idling — each of which uses energy differently from steady running. So a constant-speed range estimate at 50 kph often greatly overestimates the actual urban range. Driving cycles produce a more realistic figure for how far the vehicle will actually go.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.4.1 Driving Cycles · Page: 214
"The cycles are intended to correspond to realistic driving patterns in different conditions."
Indirect w-mod-038
Why do EV manufacturers care about the FUDS test result?
FUDS simulates typical city traffic where most EVs are used. Its result represents the kind of range customers will actually see in real urban driving. Other tests like FHDS or US06 measure highway range, while ECE-15 in Europe and US06 stand in for fast or steady cruising.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.4.1 Driving Cycles · Page: 214
"These cycles simulate urban driving, but other cycles are used to simulate out-of-town or highway driving."
Indirect w-mod-039
Why is rotor inertia more important at high gear ratios?
Motor angular acceleration is amplified by gear ratio G when reflecting back to the wheels. The force needed to accelerate the rotor scales with G²/r² times the rotor's inertia. So with a high-ratio gearbox, even a small rotor inertia can become a noticeable acceleration load.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.5 Acceleration Force · Page: 205
"The force at the wheels needed to provide the angular acceleration (Fωa) is found by combining this equation with Equation (8.5)"
Indirect w-mod-040
Why are simulations useful even before a vehicle is built?
Simulations let designers try different motor powers, battery types, masses and aerodynamics before committing to expensive hardware. You can quickly see how each change affects acceleration and range — and pick the best compromise long before metal is cut. This saves cost and shortens development.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.1 Introduction · Page: 201
"computer-based methods allow us quickly to investigate aspects of the vehicle, such as motor power, battery type and size, weight, and so on, and see how the changes might affect the performance and range."
Medium w-mod-041
How does the model handle a brushed-DC vehicle in the high-speed region?
Once the motor speed exceeds ω_c, the brushed DC motor's torque falls linearly with speed. The model substitutes this torque-speed relationship into the tractive-effort equation, giving a first-order differential equation for v that can be solved numerically with a finite-difference time step.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.3.1 Acceleration Performance Parameters · Page: 208
"In the case of a 'brushed' shunt or permanent magnet (PM) DC motor the torque falls linearly with increasing speed."
Medium w-mod-042
Why does a brushless EV operate at constant power above ω_c?
Most brushless motors are controlled so that as speed rises beyond the critical value, torque is reduced inversely with speed, keeping the product T·ω (the power) constant. This 'constant-power region' lets the vehicle keep accelerating at high speed without exceeding the motor's safe current or thermal limits.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.3.1 Acceleration Performance Parameters · Page: 208
"either the power is constant, as in most brushless type motors"
Medium w-mod-043
How is the scooter example's 250 A current limit important?
Without a current limit, the motor would draw nearly 1500 A at stall, which would damage it. The controller imposes a safe 250 A maximum (or 350 A for up to 5 seconds), which in turn fixes the maximum torque T_max for the simulation at 34 N·m.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.3.2 Modelling the Acceleration of an Electric Scooter · Page: 208
"the maximum current is controlled by the maximum safe current, in this case 250 A, so... the maximum torque T max is 34 N m."
Medium w-mod-044
How does the EV1 simulation switch behaviour at 71.3 kph?
Below 19.8 m/s (71.3 kph) the EV1 motor delivers its full 140 N·m. Above it, the motor moves into the constant-power region at 102 kW. The simulation tracks two different differential equations for the two regions and joins them when velocity reaches 19.8 m/s.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.3.3 Modelling the Acceleration of a Small Car · Page: 212
"For the motor, T max = 140 N m and ωc = 733 rad s−1 (Note that this means T = T max till v = 19.8 m s−1 (71.3 kph)). Above 19.8 m s−1 the motor operates at a constant 102 kW"
Medium w-mod-045
How does the controller cap top speed in the EV1 model?
Once velocity reaches 35.8 m/s, the EV1 controller stops any further acceleration to protect the system. The simulation models this by detecting the cap and freezing speed there. So in the velocity-versus-time plot, the curve flattens off at the controller-set top speed.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.3.3 Modelling the Acceleration of a Small Car · Page: 214
"when the velocity reaches 35.8 m −1 it stops rising, because at this point the motor controller limits any further acceleration."
Medium w-mod-046
Why isn't a single number good enough for motor efficiency in range models?
Motor efficiency varies widely with speed and torque. Range simulations spend many seconds in low-efficiency zones (low load, low speed) so using a single peak efficiency overstates the energy available. A proper model uses an efficiency map or formula η(T,ω) at every step of the cycle.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 7. Electric Machines and their Controllers · Section: 7.4.1 Improving Motor Efficiency · Page: 196
"the efficiency of an electric motor might well be very different from any figure given in the specification, if it operates well away from optimum speeds and torque."
Medium w-mod-047
How is the rolling-resistance coefficient measured in practice?
μ_rr can be found by pulling the vehicle at a steady, very low speed (so aerodynamic drag and acceleration forces are negligible) and measuring the pulling force needed. Dividing that force by the vehicle's weight gives the rolling-resistance coefficient directly.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.2.2 Rolling Resistance Force · Page: 204
"The value of μrr can reasonably readily be found by pulling a vehicle at a steady very low speed, and measuring the force required."
Medium w-mod-048
Why is performance modelling sometimes done at WOT (wide open throttle)?
Wide-open-throttle tests simulate the maximum-power demand on the system, like a hard 0–60 launch. In an electric car this means asking for maximum available torque at every step. WOT runs reveal acceleration headroom and reproduce the official 0–60 figures published by manufacturers for direct comparison.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.3.3 Modelling the Acceleration of a Small Car · Page: 212
"Above 19.8 m s−1 the motor operates at a constant 102 kW, as this is a WOT test."
Medium w-mod-049
Why is a small time step crucial in EV finite-difference simulations?
Vehicle dynamics change quickly, especially during acceleration. A small time step (e.g. 0.1 s) keeps the finite-difference approximation accurate enough to track velocity faithfully. Too large a step would over- or under-shoot the real curve. Too small a step just makes the spreadsheet slow.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.3.2 Modelling the Acceleration of an Electric Scooter · Page: 211
"Figure 8.4 is a plot of the solution using a time step δt of 0.1 seconds."
Medium w-mod-050
What can EV performance simulations reveal beyond top speed and range?
Simulations capture motor torque, speed, current and battery state at every moment. That lets designers optimise motor and gear selection, choose battery sizing, predict overheating risks, and balance trade-offs. The same data can drive component-level design decisions, not just headline 0–60 or range numbers.
Source: Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley)
Chapter: 8. Electric Vehicle Modelling · Section: 8.1 Introduction · Page: 201
"data about the motor torque and speed can be used to optimise the compromises involved in the design of the motor and other subsystems."
Basic w-des-001
What is the ideal aerodynamic shape for a vehicle?
